The Imaginary Circles of the Zodiac
Taftazani describes the imagined celestial circles dividing the zodiac.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how medieval astronomers conceptually divided the celestial sphere into twelve zodiac signs using six imaginary circles, and the naming of equinoxes, solstices, and seasons.

Core Idea
The zodiac signs are based on imagined circles, not physical divisions.

Medieval astronomy, often referenced in kalam works, relied on conceptual models to map the heavens. This passage presents the elaborate system of six intersecting circles that astronomers imagined to divide the zodiac belt into twelve equal signs. Taftazani describes this model as a prelude to theological critique, but here he simply lays out the geometry and naming conventions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full description of the six circles and their divisions.
Focus on the phrase 'توهموا' (they imagined) — these are mental constructs.
2
Identify the six circles and their role
Note that six circles intersect at the poles of the zodiac, cutting the belt into equal parts. One circle passes through the intersection points of the celestial equator and zodiac.
These circles are purely conceptual, not physical.
3
Understand the subdivisions
Each of the twelve resulting sections is called a 'sign' (برج), subdivided into degrees, minutes, seconds, etc.
The system is used for tracking celestial motions.
4
Learn the equinoxes and solstices
The sun's crossing points define the equinoxes and solstices, which mark the beginning of seasons. The passage names them and assigns the zodiac signs to seasons.
Vernal equinox: sun moves north; autumnal: south. Summer/winter solstices are midpoints.
5
Note the origin of sign names
The names of the signs come from star formations that originally aligned, but were kept for convenience even after precession shifted them.
This shows the practical nature of astronomical naming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث
توهمهم الدوائر المتقاطعة على قطبي البرج
قال (المبحث الثالث)
توهموا ست دوائر متقاطعة على قطبي البرج قاطعاً لمنطقتها على أبعاد سواء مارة إحداها بنقطتي تقاطع المعدل، ومنطقة البروج، ثم فرضوها قاطعة للعالم، فانقسم الفلك الأعظم وسائر الممثلات أيضاً، اثني عشر قسماً، سموا كل قسم برجاً، وجعلوا كل برج ثلاثين درجة، وكل درجة ستين دقيقة، وكل دقيقة ستين ثانية، وهكذا، وسموا نقطة التقاطع التي تجاوزها الشمس إلى شمال المعدل، اعتدالاً ربيعياً، وإلى جنوبه اعتدالاً خريفياً، ومنتصف ما بين نقطتي التقاطع في الشمال انقلاباً صيفياً، وفي الجنوب انقلاباً شتوياً، وزمان قطع الشمس من البروج الشمالية الحمل، والثور، والجوزاء، ربيعاً، والسرطان والميزان والأسد والسنبلة صيفاً، ومن الجنوب العقرب والقوس خريفاً، والجدي والدلو والحوت شتاء.
لما كانت منطقة البروج ومعدل النهار متقاطعين على نقطتين، توهموا دائرة تمر بنقطتي التقاطع، ونقطتي البروج، وأخرى تمر بالأقطاب الأربعة، وتقطع معدل النهار ومنطقة البروج على زوايا قوائم فتقع على غاية بعد ما بين المنطقتين، ويسمى الميل الكلي، فبحسب هاتين الدائرتين ينقسم فلك البروج أربعة أقسام متساوية، فتوهموا على كل واحد من ربعين متلاصقين منها نقطتين، بعد كل منهما عن الأخرى كبعد الأخرى عن أقرب طرفي الربع إليها. ثم توهموا أربع دوائر تمر بالنقط الأربع، وبمقابلاتها من الربعين الباقيين، وفرضوا الدوائر الست قاطعة للعالم، فانقسم الفلك الأعظم، وسائر الأفلاك الممثلة اثني عشر قسماً، سموا كل قسم منها، برجاً، وجعلوا كل برج ثلاثين قسماً، سموا كل قسم درجة، وكل درجة ستين قسماً سموا كل قسم دقيقة، وهكذا، جعلوا كل دقيقة ستين ثانية، وكل ثانية ستين ثالثة بالغاً ما بلغ، وسموا من نقطتي تقاطع المنطقتين النقطة التي تجاوزها الشمس إلى شمال معدل النهار، أعني مائل القطب الشمالي اعتدالاً ربيعياً، والنقطة التي تجاوزها الشمس إلى جنوب المعدل اعتدالاً خريفياً، وسموا نقطة تقاطع منطقة البروج، والدائرة المارة بالأقطاب الأربعة في جانب الشمال انقلاباً صيفياً، وفي جانب الجنوب، انقلاباً شتوياً، لأن الربيع اسم لمدة حركة الشمس من الاعتدال الآخذ في الشمال إلى الانقلاب الشمالي، أعني زمان قطعها للحمل والثور والجوزاء، والصيف لمدة حركتها منه إلى الاعتدال الآخذ في الجنوب، أعني زمان قطعها للسرطان والأسد والسنبلة، والخريف لمدة حركتها منه إلى الانقلاب الجنوبي، أعني زمان قطعها للميزان والعقرب والقوس، والشتاء لمدة حركتها منه إلى الاعتدال الربيعي أعني زمان قطعها للجدي والدلو والحوت، وهذه الأسامي مأخوذة من صور توهمت من كواكب وقعت عند القسمة بحذاء الأقسام - وحين انتقلت عن محاذاتها بحركة الفلك الثامن آثروا بقاء الأسماء تيسيراً للأمر في ضبط الحركات.
The third section: Their imagining of intersecting circles on the poles of the zodiac. He said (the third section): They imagined six circles intersecting on the poles of the zodiac, cutting its belt at equal distances, one of them passing through the two points of intersection of the celestial equator and the zodiac belt. Then they supposed them cutting the world, so the greatest sphere and the other representative spheres also divided into twelve sections. They named each section a sign (burj), and made each sign thirty degrees, each degree sixty minutes, each minute sixty seconds, and so on. They named the point of intersection which the sun passes to the north of the equator the vernal equinox, and to its south the autumnal equinox; the midpoint between the two points of intersection in the north the summer solstice, and in the south the winter solstice. The period of the sun's passage through the northern signs—Aries, Taurus, Gemini—is spring; through Cancer, Leo, Virgo—summer; through Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius—autumn; and through Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces—winter. Since the zodiac belt and the celestial equator intersect at two points, they imagined a circle passing through the two points of intersection and the two points of the zodiac, and another passing through the four poles, cutting the celestial equator and the zodiac belt at right angles, falling at the farthest distance between the two belts, called the total declination. According to these two circles, the zodiac sphere is divided into four equal parts. Then they imagined on each of two adjacent quarters two points, each at a distance from the other equal to the distance of the other from the nearer end of the quarter. Then they imagined four circles passing through the four points and their counterparts in the remaining two quarters, and supposed the six circles cutting the world, so the greatest sphere and the other representative spheres divided into twelve sections. They named each section a sign, and made each sign thirty parts, each part a degree, each degree sixty parts, each part a minute, and so on, making each minute sixty seconds, each second sixty thirds, and so on indefinitely. They named from the two points of intersection of the two belts the point which the sun passes to the north of the celestial equator—i.e., the declination of the north pole—the vernal equinox, and the point which the sun passes to the south of the equator the autumnal equinox. They named the point of intersection of the zodiac belt and the circle passing through the four poles in the north the summer solstice, and in the south the winter solstice. Because spring is the name for the period of the sun's motion from the equinox heading north to the northern solstice, i.e., the time of its passing through Aries, Taurus, and Gemini; summer for the period of its motion from there to the equinox heading south, i.e., the time of its passing through Cancer, Leo, and Virgo; autumn for the period of its motion from there to the southern solstice, i.e., the time of its passing through Libra, Scorpio, and Sagittarius; winter for the period of its motion from there to the vernal equinox, i.e., the time of its passing through Capricorn, Aquarius, and Pisces. These names are taken from imagined forms of stars that fell at the time of division opposite the sections—and when they moved from their alignment by the motion of the eighth sphere, they preferred to keep the names for ease of regulating the motions.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the astronomical model imagined by astrologers/astronomers (often referenced in kalam works for refutation). It describes how they conceived six intersecting circles on the poles of the zodiac to divide the celestial sphere into twelve equal parts (the signs of the zodiac). The passage first explains that they imagined six circles cutting the zodiac belt at equal distances, with one circle passing through the two intersection points of the celestial equator and the zodiac. They then supposed these circles cut the entire universe, dividing the greatest sphere into twelve sections called 'burj' (signs). Each sign was subdivided into 30 degrees, each degree into 60 minutes, etc. They named the equinoxes and solstices based on the sun's position. The vernal equinox is where the sun crosses to the north of the equator, the autumnal to the south; the summer solstice is the midpoint between equinoxes in the north, winter solstice in the south. They assigned the seasons to the sun's passage through specific signs: spring through Aries, Taurus, Gemini; summer through Cancer, Leo, Virgo; autumn through Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius; winter through Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces. The passage then elaborates on the geometry: since the zodiac belt and celestial equator intersect at two points, they imagined a circle through those intersection points and another through the four poles (the ecliptic's poles?), cutting at right angles, defining the total declination. These two circles divide the zodiac sphere into four equal quarters. On two adjacent quarters, they imagined two points, then drew four circles through those points and their counterparts, resulting in six circles that cut the world into twelve sections again. They further subdivided each sign into degrees, minutes, seconds, thirds, ad infinitum. The names of the signs were taken from imagined star formations that originally aligned with those sections, but even after the stars shifted due to the precession of the eighth sphere, they retained the names for convenience. The author (Taftazani) is likely presenting this model as a prelude to critiquing it from an Islamic theological perspective, but here he simply describes what the astronomers imagined.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these circles are real celestial entities.
Correction: The passage repeatedly uses 'توهموا' (they imagined), indicating these are conceptual constructs for mapping, not physical structures.

Checkpoint
How many imaginary circles did they suppose intersect at the poles of the zodiac?
A) 4
B) 6
C) 8
D) 12

Key Takeaway
The twelve zodiac signs and their subdivisions originate from a conceptual model of six imaginary circles, designed for astronomical calculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat any human-made classifications (like social status, ethnicity, or definitions of success) as if they were absolute realities, similar to how people may treat the zodiac signs? Are you willing to see them as conceptual constructs and re-evaluate your attachment to them in light of truth?
?
The passage notes that the names of the signs were kept 'for ease of regulating motions' even after the stars shifted. In your life, do you hold onto convenient labels or traditions that no longer align with truth or reality? What would it take for you to let go?
?
When you encounter a widely accepted idea (like the twelve zodiac signs), do you ever pause to ask: 'Is this based on reality or merely an imagined convenience?' How can you cultivate the courage to question such constructs in your own faith and daily judgments?

General Motion of Planets Through the Zodiac
Al-Taftazani defines general planetary motion and lists approximate orbital periods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of general motion as applied to planets and know the approximate time each planet takes to traverse the zodiac.

Core Idea
Planets move west to east through the zodiac at different rates, approximately given.

Al-Taftazani introduces the idea of 'general motion', a simplified view of planetary movement focusing only on sign-to-sign transitions. He then provides approximate orbital periods for each planet, noting that precise figures are found in astronomical tables.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the repeated phrases and the clarifications.
The passage has some repetition; the second part clarifies the first.
2
Identify the key concept: general motion
Look for the definition of 'general motion' (al-haraka al-ijmaliyya). It is the composite motion that considers only the transition from one zodiac sign to the next, ignoring details of spheres.
3
List the planets and their periods
Scan the passage for the approximate periods of the Sun, Moon, Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Venus, and Mercury. Write them down for reference.
The periods are approximate, as stated by the author.
4
Understand the direction and order
Note that the motion is from west to east, in successive order from Aries to Taurus, etc. The opposite direction is also mentioned.
5
Contrast with detailed motion
The author contrasts general motion with detailed motion (bi al-tafṣīl), which will be discussed later. This sets up a distinction important for later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطع الكواكب السيارة لمنطقة البروج
قال وكل من السيارة يقطع هذه البروج على التوالي) أعني بحركة من المغرب إلى المشرق. إما إجمالاً فالشمس في ثلثمائة وخمسة وستين يوماً وربع يوم وهي السنة الشمسية، والقمر في ثلثمائة وأربعة وخمسين يوماً وسدس يوم وخمس . وزحل في ثلاثين سنة . والمشتري في اثنتي عشرة سنة، والمريخ في سنتين إلا شهراً ونصفاً والزهرة في حدود سنة، وكذا عطارد كل ذلك على التقريب] .
أعني من الحمل إلى الثور إلى الجوزاء ،وهكذا، وهي حركة من المغرب إلى المشرق وعكسه بخلاف التوالي أعني من الحمل إلى الحوت إلى الدلو، وهكذا، ونعني بالحركة إجمالاً الحركة المركبة التي بها يعتبر انتقال الكوكب من برج إلى برج من غير أن ينظر إلى جزئيات الأفلاك وتفاصيل الحركات . وبالتفصيل خلاف ذلك. فالشمس تقطع البروج الاثني عشر في ثلثمائة وخمسة وستين يوماً وربع يوم، وهي السنة الشمسية والقمر [يقطعها اثنتي عشرة مرة] السنة القمرية، ثلثمائة وأربعة وخمسين يوماً وسدس يوم وخمس يوم وهي في وزحل في ثلاثين سنة، والمشتري في اثنتي عشرة سنة، والمريخ في سنتين إلا شهراً ونصفاً، وكل من الزهرة وعطارد في سنة ذلك على سبيل التقريب جميع وأما التحقيق فيعرف من الزيجات .
The cutting of the wandering planets for the zodiac belt. He said: 'And each of the wandering planets cuts these signs successively' – meaning by motion from west to east. As a summary, the sun in 365 and a quarter days, which is the solar year; the moon in 354 days, a sixth day, and a fifth; Saturn in 30 years; Jupiter in 12 years; Mars in 2 years except a month and a half; Venus in about a year, and likewise Mercury – all approximately. Meaning from Aries to Taurus to Gemini, and so on, and it is a motion from west to east, and its opposite contrary to succession – meaning from Aries to Pisces to Aquarius, and so on. And we mean by motion 'summary' the composite motion by which the transition of the planet from one sign to another is considered, without looking at the particulars of the spheres and details of motions. And in detail, it is different. So the sun cuts the twelve signs in 365 and a quarter days, which is the solar year; and the moon [cuts them twelve times] the lunar year, 354 days, a sixth day, and a fifth day – and it is; Saturn in 30 years; Jupiter in 12 years; Mars in 2 years except a month and a half; and each of Venus and Mercury in a year – all approximately. As for the exact, it is known from the zijes.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage defines the general (ijmali) motion of the planets through the zodiac belt. It begins by stating that each planet cuts (i.e., traverses) the signs successively, from west to east. Then it gives approximate periods for each planet: the sun in about 365.25 days (solar year), the moon in about 354.2 days (lunar year), Saturn in 30 years, Jupiter in 12 years, Mars in about 2 years minus a month and a half, Venus and Mercury in about a year. The author clarifies that by 'general motion' he means the composite motion that considers only the transition from sign to sign, ignoring the detailed motions of the spheres. He contrasts this with detailed motion, which will be discussed later. The passage ends by noting that exact figures are found in astronomical tables (zijes).

Correction: The passage states that the periods are approximate ('ala al-taqrib) and that exact calculations require astronomical tables (al-zijat).

A) 12 years

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage tells us that the lunar year is about 10 days shorter than the solar year, which directly affects the timing of Ramadan and other acts of worship. Do you see this difference as a test of your obedience to Allah's appointed times, or do you simply follow the calendar without reflecting on His precise ordering?
?
Al-Taftazani presents these planetary periods as approximate, reminding us that exact knowledge belongs to Allah alone. In your life, do you humbly acknowledge the limits of your knowledge, or do you cling to approximations as if they were certainties, forgetting that Allah's knowledge is perfect and encompasses all?
?
The planets move with a regularity that points to a wise Creator. When you witness the sunrise or the phases of the moon, does awe overwhelm you, or have you become numb to these signs? What would change if you reminded yourself daily that each motion is by Allah's command?

Detailed Simple Motions of the Celestial Spheres
Al-Taftazani details the simple motions of individual celestial spheres with specific numbers and a textual criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific numerical values for the simple motions of the celestial spheres in Ptolemaic astronomy as presented by Al-Taftazani and identify a textual error noted by him.

Core Idea
The celestial spheres have precise simple motions measured in minutes and seconds.

This passage presents the detailed (tafsili) explanation of the simple motions of the partial spheres, a core topic in Islamic astronomy. Al-Taftazani lists the daily motion of the director, the motions of eccentrics for each planet, and the epicycle motions, all with exact figures. He also corrects a slip in the work of a previous scholar.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of detailed simple motions
Recognize that this passage follows a general introduction and now provides specific numerical values for the motions of individual spheres.
The term 'simple motions' refers to the basic rotations that combine to produce planetary appearances in the Ptolemaic model.
2
Identify the diurnal motion and the director of Mercury
Read the first part describing the motion contrary to succession and how the 'director' (al-mudir) of Mercury exemplifies it. Note the precise angular speed: 59 minutes, 8 seconds, 20 thirds per day.
The director is an imaginary sphere producing the daily rotation.
3
Learn the eccentric and epicyclic motions for the planets
Go through the list of values for eccentric motions (sun, Saturn, Mars, Venus, Mercury, moon) and epicyclic motions (superior planets, Venus, Mercury, moon). Note the special case of the moon's epicycle.
These numbers are based on observations and models from Ptolemy's Almagest.
4
Note the textual criticism and the focus on Mercury's director
The passage concludes by focusing again on Mercury's director, explaining its motion in more detail, and points out a slip in the transmission of the author of al-Mawaqif.
The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the original text continues beyond this excerpt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركات البسيطة للأفلاك الجزئية
قال (وأما تفصيلاً)
فحركة اليوم بليلته إلى خلاف التوالي، أعني من المشرق إلى المغرب كمدير عطارد، وعلى غير منطقة العالم، ومنطقة البروج، وأقطابها تسع وخمسون دقيقة، وثماني ثوان وعشرون ثالثة، ولممثله على منطقة البروج وقطبيها ثلاث دقائق وكسر، ولمائله على غير المنطقتين والأقطاب إحدى عشرة درجة، وتسع دقائق و إلى التوالي لممثلات غير القمر على وفق الثامن، حتى كأنها بحركة، ولخارج المركز للشمس على منطقة البروج دون قطبيها تسع وخمسون دقيقة، وثماني ثوان وعشرون ثالثة، ولزحل دقيقتان، وللمريخ إحدى وثلاثون دقيقة، وللزهرة كالشمس، ولعطارد درجة ونصف، وللقمر أربع وعشرون درجة وثلث، وعشرون دقيقة، كل ذلك على غير المنطقتين، والأقطاب ولتداوير العلوية فضل حركة الشمس على حواملها ، ولتدوير الزهرة سبع وثلاثون دقيقة، ولعطارد ثلاثة أجزاء وست دقائق، والقمر ثلاثة عشر جزءاً وأربع دقائق، وهذه في تداوير المتحيرة للنصف الأعلى، وعلى غير مناطق الحوامل، وفي تدوير القمر للنصف الأسفل، وعلى منطقة الحامل والمائل، فلا محالة يكون النصف الآخر إلى خلاف التوالي. إشارة إلى بيان الحركات البسيطة للأفلاك الجزئية إلى التوالي، أو خلافه، فمن
الحركة إلى خلاف التوالي حركة مدير عطارد حول مركزه على غير معدل النهار ومنطقة البروج ، وغير أقطابهما ، ويظهر في أوج الحامل، وحضيضه، ويحدث بسببها لمركز الحامل مدار حول مركز المدير يسمى الفلك الحامل لمركز الحامل وهي في اليوم بليلته تسع وخمسون دقيقة، وثماني ثوان وعشرون ثالثة، ووجه معرفة المدير، وكون حركته على خلاف التوالي، وعلى هذا المقدار مشهور فيما بينهم. إلا أن في نقل صاحب المواقف سهو قلم لا بد من التنبه له، وهو أنه
The simple motions of the partial spheres. He said: 'And as for in detail': the motion of day and night contrary to succession, meaning from east to west, like the director of Mercury, and upon other than the celestial equator and the zodiac belt, and their poles: fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, and twenty thirds; and for its representative upon the zodiac and its poles: three minutes and a fraction; and for its inclined upon other than the two belts and poles: eleven degrees and nine minutes; and to succession for representatives other than the moon according to the eighth, as if it is by motion; and for the eccentric of the sun upon the zodiac without its poles: fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, and twenty thirds; and for Saturn: two minutes; for Mars: thirty-one minutes; for Venus as the sun; for Mercury: one and a half degrees; for the moon: twenty-four degrees and a third, and twenty minutes; all that upon other than the two belts and the poles; and for the epicycles of the superior planets: the excess of the sun's motion over their carriers; and for the epicycle of Venus: thirty-seven minutes; for Mercury: three parts and six minutes; and the moon: thirteen parts and four minutes; and these are in the epicycles of the wandering planets for the upper half, and upon other than the regions of the carriers; and in the epicycle of the moon for the lower half, and upon the region of the carrier and the inclined; so necessarily the other half is contrary to succession. An indication of the explanation of the simple motions of the partial spheres to succession or its opposite. So from the motion contrary to succession: the motion of the director of Mercury around its center upon other than the celestial equator and the zodiac belt, and other than their poles, and it appears at the apogee of the carrier and its perigee, and due to it for the center of the carrier an orbit around the center of the director called the sphere carrying the center of the carrier, and it is in day and night: fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, and twenty thirds; and the way of knowing the director and that its motion is contrary to succession, and upon this amount is well-known among them. Except that in the transmission of the author of al-Mawaqif there is a slip of the pen which must be noted, and that is...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage begins the detailed (tafsili) explanation of the simple motions of the partial celestial spheres. The author first describes the diurnal motion contrary to succession (from east to west) as exemplified by the director (al-mudir) of Mercury. He gives precise angular measures for various motions: the precession of the equinoxes (59 minutes, 8 seconds, 20 thirds per day), the motion of the representative (mumaththil) on the zodiac (3 minutes and a fraction), the inclined (mail) on other planes (11 degrees 9 minutes). Then he gives values for the eccentric (kharij al-markaz) of each planet: sun (same as precession), Saturn (2 minutes), Mars (31 minutes), Venus (like sun), Mercury (1.5 degrees), moon (24 degrees 20 minutes). For epicycles (tadawir), he gives the excess motion of the sun over the carriers for superior planets, and specific values for Venus (37 minutes), Mercury (3 parts 6 minutes), and the moon (13 parts 4 minutes). He notes that for the upper half of the epicycle the motion is to succession, for the lower half contrary. Then he returns to explain the motion of Mercury's director in detail, noting that its period is about 59 minutes 8 seconds 20 thirds per day, and that this is well-known. He ends by noting a slip in the transmission of the author of al-Mawaqif (Qadi al-Baydawi?).

The Director of Mercury and the Correction of al-Mawaqif
Enumeration of motions against the order and correction of a mistake in al-Mawaqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of simple motions against the order and the specific parameters of Mercury's director motion, as well as the correction to a mistake in al-Mawaqif.

Core Idea
Mercury's director motion exemplifies motion against the order, and even scholars can err in astronomical details.

Al-Taftazani begins by enumerating the simple motions of the partial spheres that go against the order of signs. Among these, the motion of Mercury's director is highlighted with precise angular measurements. In this context, he corrects a significant mistake made by the author of al-Mawaqif, offering a valuable lesson in careful astronomical observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the technical terms and the flow of the enumeration.
Pay attention to the values given for each motion.
2
Identify Mercury's director motion
Note the parameters: 59 minutes, 8 seconds, 20 thirds per day, and that it moves against the order on a plane distinct from the equator and ecliptic.
3
Understand the correction
Compare al-Taftazani's statement with the error in al-Mawaqif: the apogee and epicycle center are in conjunction, not opposition; the director moves the apogee, not the epicycle center; Mercury's distance is greater in Aries than in Libra.
4
Reflect on the broader enumeration
Notice that other motions (representatives, eccentrics, epicycles) are listed with their values, all against the order or with the order as specified.
This shows the complexity of celestial motions.

وعشرون ثالثة ولممثله على منطقة البروج وقطبيها ثلاث دقائق وكسر ولمائله على غير المنطقتين والأقطاب إحدى عشرة درجة وتسع دقائق و إلى التوالي لممثلات غير القمر على وفق الثامن حتى كأنها بحركة ولخارج المركز للشمس على منطقة البروج دون قطبيها تسع وخمسون دقيقة وثماني ثوان وعشرون ثالثة ولزحل دقيقتان وللمريخ إحدى وثلاثون دقيقة وللزهرة كالشمس ولعطارد درجة ونصف وللقمر أربع وعشرون درجة وثلث وعشرون دقيقة كل ذلك على غير المنطقتين والأقطاب ولتداوير العلوية فضل حركة الشمس على حواملها ولتدوير الزهرة سبع وثلاثون دقيقة ولعطارد ثلاثة أجزاء وست دقائق والقمر ثلاثة عشر جزءاً وأربع دقائق وهذه في تداوير المتحيرة للنصف الأعلى وعلى غير مناطق الحوامل وفي تدوير القمر للنصف الأسفل وعلى منطقة الحامل والمائل فلا محالة يكون النصف الآخر إلى خلاف التوالي إشارة إلى بيان الحركات البسيطة للأفلاك الجزئية إلى التوالي أو خلافه فمن الحركة إلى خلاف التوالي حركة مدير عطارد حول مركزه على غير معدل النهار ومنطقة البروج وغير أقطابهما ويظهر في أوج الحامل وحضيضه ويحدث بسببها لمركز الحامل مدار حول مركز المدير يسمى الفلك الحامل لمركز الحامل وهي في اليوم بليلته تسع وخمسون دقيقة وثماني ثوان وعشرون ثالثة ووجه معرفة المدير وكون حركته على خلاف التوالي وعلى هذا المقدار مشهور فيما بينهم إلا أن في نقل صاحب المواقف سهو قلم لا بد من التنبه له وهو أنه جعل حال مركز التدوير وأوج الحامل في الميزان والحمل المقابلة وإنما هي المقارنة وجعل المدير إسماً لمحرك مركز التدوير وإنما هو اسم لمحرك أوج الحامل المحرك لمركز التدوير وجعل بعد عطارد عن الشمس في الصباح والمساء في الميزان أعظم منه في الحمل والأمر بالعكس ومنها حركة ممثل القمر حول مركز العالم على منطقة البروج وقطبيها كل يوم ثلاث دقائق واثنتي عشرة ثانية وبها تتحرك جميع أفلاك القمر فينتقل الرأس والذنب ولذلك ينسب إليهما ويسمى حركة الجوزهر ومنها حركة مائل القمر حول مركز العالم على غير معدل النهار ومنطقة البروج وغير أقطابهما كل يوم إحدى عشرة درجة وتسع دقائق ويتحرك بها الخارج المركز ومركزه وتسمى حركة الأوج لظهورها فيه وقد يسمى مجموع حركة الجوزهر والمائل بحركة الأوج
The simple motions of the partial spheres. He said: 'As for detail, the daily motion in its night is against the order, meaning from east to west, like the director of Mercury, and not on the plane of the equator nor the ecliptic, and their poles, fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, and twenty thirds. For its representative on the ecliptic and its poles, three minutes and a fraction. For its inclined, on neither of the two planes nor the poles, eleven degrees and nine minutes. And with the order for the representatives other than the moon's representative, according to the eighth sphere, as if they were by its motion. For the eccentric of the sun on the ecliptic without its poles, fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, twenty thirds. For Saturn, two minutes. For Mars, thirty-one minutes. For Venus, like the sun. For Mercury, one and a half degrees. For the moon, twenty-four degrees and a third, and twenty minutes. All that on neither of the two planes nor the poles. For the epicycles of the superior planets, the excess of the sun's motion over their centers. For the epicycle of Venus, thirty-seven minutes. For Mercury, three parts and six minutes. For the moon, thirteen parts and four minutes. These in the epicycles of the wandering planets are for the upper half, and not on the planes of the centers. In the epicycle of the moon, for the lower half, and on the plane of the center and the inclined. So inevitably the other half is against the order. Indication of the explanation of the simple motions of the partial spheres with the order or against it. So among the motions against the order is the motion of the director of Mercury around its center, not on the equator nor the ecliptic nor their poles. It appears at the apogee and perigee of the center. Because of it, for the center of the center, an orbit around the center of the director arises, called the sphere carrying the center of the center. It is in the day and its night fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, twenty thirds. The way of knowing the director and that its motion is against the order, and this amount is well-known among them, except that in the transmission of the author of al-Mawaqif there is a slip of the pen that must be noted, which is that he placed the state of the center of the epicycle and the apogee of the center in Libra and the opposition of Aries, but it is conjunction. He made 'director' a name for the mover of the center of the epicycle, whereas it is a name for the mover of the apogee of the center that moves the center of the epicycle. He made the distance of Mercury from the sun in the morning and evening in Libra greater than in Aries, but the opposite is true. And among them is the motion of the representative of the moon around the center of the world on the ecliptic and its poles, every day three minutes and twelve seconds. By it all the spheres of the moon move, so the head and tail are transferred, and therefore they are attributed to it, and it is called the motion of the jawzahar. And among them is the motion of the inclined of the moon around the center of the world, not on the equator nor the ecliptic nor their poles, every day eleven degrees and nine minutes. By it the eccentric and its center move, and it is called the motion of the apogee because it appears in it. And sometimes the sum of the motion of the jawzahar and the inclined is called the motion of the apogee.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage enumerates the simple motions of the partial spheres (الحركات البسيطة للأفلاك الجزئية) that are against the order of signs (خلاف التوالي). It begins with the daily motion of Mercury's director (مدير عطارد), specifying its parameters: fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, twenty thirds per day, on a plane different from the equator and ecliptic. Then it gives values for the representative (ممثل) and inclined (مائل) motions for various planets, noting that the representatives of planets other than the moon move with the order according to the eighth sphere. For the eccentrics (خارج المركز), it lists values for the sun, Saturn, Mars, Venus, Mercury, and the moon. It then discusses the epicycles (تداوير) of the superior planets and the moon, explaining that the upper half moves with the order and the lower half against the order. A key point is the correction of a mistake in al-Mawaqif: the author (al-Iji) wrongly placed the center of the epicycle and the apogee in Libra and opposition in Aries, whereas they are in conjunction; he misnamed the director as the mover of the epicycle center instead of the mover of the apogee; and he claimed Mercury's distance from the sun is greater in Libra than in Aries, but the opposite is true. Finally, it describes the moon's representative motion (حركة ممثل القمر) which causes the nodes (الجوزهر), and the moon's inclined motion (حركة مائل القمر) which causes the apogee motion, sometimes collectively called the motion of the apogee. All these motions are against the order.

خلاف التوالي
مدير عطارد
ممثل
مائل
خارج المركز
تدوير
أوج
حامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The director of Mercury moves the center of the epicycle.
Correction: The director actually moves the apogee of the center, which in turn moves the epicycle center.

Checkpoint
What mistake did the author of al-Mawaqif make according to al-Taftazani?
A) He confused the motion of Mercury with that of Venus.
B) He placed the apogee in Libra and the opposition in Aries, when they are actually in conjunction.
C) He said the director moves with the sun.
D) He claimed the moon's motion is faster.

Key Takeaway
The director of Mercury moves against the order with a daily motion of 59 minutes, 8 seconds, 20 thirds, and al-Taftazani corrects al-Mawaqif's errors regarding the apogee and the director's function.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you realize that a respected scholar like al-Iji made a slip of the pen in a field outside his specialization, does it humble you to think of your own areas of ignorance? In what specific area of knowledge do you speak with unwarranted certainty, and how can you begin to correct that today?
?
This lesson required al-Taftazani to point out a small but significant error. When someone corrects you in a matter you thought you knew, do you feel defensive or grateful? Reflect on a time you were corrected and what your immediate reaction revealed about your heart.
?
The passage carefully distinguishes between the director as mover of the apogee versus the epicycle center—a subtle but crucial difference. In your own life, where are you substituting an imprecise understanding for the truth? What step will you take this week to seek deeper accuracy in that area?

The Moon's Motions: Representative, Inclined, and Epicycles
Description of the moon's simple motions: representative and inclined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the moon's representative motion (jawzahar) and inclined motion (apogee), and how the epicyclic motion results in opposite directions for the upper and lower halves.

Core Idea
The moon's motions are split into representative and inclined, which together produce its complex movement and impact on nodes and apogee.

Al-Taftazani now focuses on the moon's unique simple motions. The moon's representative motion, also called the motion of the jawzahar (nodes), moves daily by three minutes and twelve seconds. Its inclined motion, which drives the apogee, is much faster at eleven degrees and nine minutes per day. These motions are crucial for understanding the moon's orbit and its nodes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the moon's motions
Focus on the two distinct motions: representative and inclined. Note their daily rates and effects.
2
Understand the term 'jawzahar'
The representative motion causes the nodes (head and tail) to move, so it is called the motion of the jawzahar.
3
Understand the term 'apogee motion'
The inclined motion moves the eccentric and its center, and is called the motion of the apogee because it appears there.
Sometimes the sum of both motions is called the motion of the apogee.
4
Relate to the epicycle discussion
Recall from lesson 1 that the moon's epicycle has the lower half moving against the order, while the upper half moves with the order.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moon's apogee motion is solely due to the inclined motion.
Correction: The passage states that sometimes the sum of both representative and inclined motions is called the motion of the apogee, indicating a combined effect.

Checkpoint
What is the daily rate of the moon's representative motion?
A) 3 minutes and 12 seconds
B) 11 degrees and 9 minutes
C) 59 minutes and 8 seconds
D) 24 degrees and 20 minutes

Key Takeaway
The moon's motions are twofold: the representative (jawzahar) moves the nodes, and the inclined (apogee) moves the apogee, with daily rates of 3 minutes 12 seconds and 11 degrees 9 minutes respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals two precise motions of the moon, each with specific rates. If you truly believe that Allah is the All-Knowing Creator, why do you spend so little time contemplating the minute details of His creation? How can you change your daily routine to include reflection on the celestial signs?
?
The moon's motions are 'against the order' from a human perspective, yet they follow a divine order. In your own life, where do you resist trusting Allah's plan because it seems disorderly to you? What step can you take today to surrender to that which you do not understand?
?
You have learned that the inclined motion moves the apogee. How often do you consider your own 'apogee'—the farthest you feel from Allah? What does the moon's consistent return from its apogee teach you about repentance and hope? What will you do this week to turn back to Him?

Motions with the Order: Representative and Eccentric Spheres
Al-Taftazani enumerates motions with the order for two sphere types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of simple motions with the order (representative and eccentric spheres) and the disagreement over the cause of the representative spheres' motion.

Core Idea
Heavenly spheres move 'with the order' in debated causation and precise daily rates.

This lesson explores the simple motions that occur 'with the order' (إلى التوالي), focusing on two types: representative spheres (except the moon's) and eccentric spheres (except Mercury's director). Al-Taftazani presents the disagreement about whether the representative spheres move by the eighth sphere or by their own motion, and then provides the daily motion amounts for each planet on the eccentric spheres.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the two categories and the numerical data.
Notice the two main parts: representative spheres and eccentric spheres.
2
Understand the representative sphere motion
Focus on the description of representative spheres, their relation to the eighth sphere, and the two opinions about their cause. Ask: Why do some scholars insist on self-motion?
The key term 'عطل' (idle) explains their reasoning.
3
Note the daily values for eccentric spheres
Identify the daily motion amounts for each planet on the eccentric spheres. Notice the units: degrees, minutes, seconds, thirds.
Compare the speeds: the moon is fastest, Saturn slowest.
4
Reflect on the plane specification
The passage ends by stating all these motions are on neither of the two principal planes (equator and ecliptic) nor their poles. Consider why this detail matters.

وأما حركاتها إلى التوالي فمنها حركات الأفلاك الممثلة سوى ممثل القمر وتظهر في الأوجات والحضيضات وهي على وفق حركة الفلك الثامن حتى ذهب بعضهم إلى أنها بحركته واختار الآخرون كونها حركات الممثلات بذواتها احترازاً عن كونها عطلاً والتزموا عدم تحركها بحركة الثامن لأن تحريك الحاوي للمحوي ليس بلازم ومنها حركات الأفلاك الخارج المراكز سوى ما يخص باسم المدير لعطارد وهي في كل يوم للشمس تسع وخمسون دقيقة وثماني ثوان وعشرون ثالثة على منطقة البروج دون قطبيها ولزحل دقيقتان والمشتري خمس دقائق وللمريخ إحدى وثلاثون دقيقة وللزهرة كما للشمس ولعطارد درجة ونصف وللقمر أربع وعشرون درجة وثلث وعشرون دقيقة كل ذلك على غير المنطقتين والأقطاب
And as for their motions with the order, among them are the motions of the representative spheres except the moon's representative. They appear at the apogees and perigees, and they are according to the motion of the eighth sphere, such that some went to that they are by its motion, and others chose that they are self-motions of the representatives, to avoid their being idle. They insisted that they are not moved by the motion of the eighth, because moving the contained by the container is not necessary. And among them are the motions of the eccentric spheres except what is specifically called the director of Mercury. For the sun, every day, fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, twenty thirds, on the ecliptic without its poles. For Saturn, two minutes. For Jupiter, five minutes. For Mars, thirty-one minutes. For Venus, as for the sun. For Mercury, one and a half degrees. For the moon, twenty-four degrees and a third and twenty minutes. All that on neither of the two planes nor the poles.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the simple motions that are with the order (إلى التوالي), completing the enumeration. It first discusses the motions of the representative spheres (الأفلاك الممثلة) other than the moon's. These appear at apogees and perigees and follow the motion of the eighth sphere. A disagreement is noted: some argue that these motions are caused by the eighth sphere itself, while others hold that the representatives have their own self-motions, to avoid being idle (عطل). The latter group insists that the representative spheres are not moved by the eighth sphere because it is not necessary for a container to impart motion to its contents. The passage then lists the motions of the eccentric spheres (الأفلاك الخارج المراكز), excluding the specific case of Mercury's director. For each planet, a daily amount is given: the sun (59 min, 8 sec, 20 thirds), Saturn (2 min), Jupiter (5 min), Mars (31 min), Venus (same as sun), Mercury (1.5 degrees), and the moon (24 degrees and a third, plus 20 minutes). All these motions are on planes other than the two principal planes (equator and ecliptic) and their poles.

أفلاك خارج المراكز
الفلك الثامن
أوج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The representative spheres are necessarily moved by the eighth sphere.
Correction: Some scholars argue that the representative spheres move by themselves to avoid being idle, as the container (eighth sphere) does not necessarily move its contents.

Moon's Two Motions: Representative and Inclined Spheres
Description of the moon's two motions: the representative sphere and the inclined sphere

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct motions of the moon: the motion of its representative sphere (node motion) and the motion of its inclined sphere (apogee motion), along with their rates and effects.

Core Idea
The moon has two motions: one slow (nodes) and one fast (apogee).

This passage from the text describes the two distinct motions of the moon's spheres. Understanding these motions is crucial for predicting the moon's position and for grasping the complexity of the celestial model. The author presents both motions with their specific rates and effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the two motions described.
Notice the technical terms for each motion.
2
Identify the two motions
The first motion is of the representative sphere (ممثل القمر) moving on the ecliptic at 3 minutes 12 seconds per day. The second is of the inclined sphere (مائل القمر) moving not on the ecliptic at 11 degrees 9 minutes per day.
Pay attention to the different rates and axes.
3
Understand the effects
The first motion moves the nodes (head and tail) and is called motion of the nodes (حركة الجوزهر). The second moves the eccentric and its center and is called motion of the apogee (حركة الأوج). Sometimes both are summed under motion of the apogee.
Think about why these motions are important for lunar calculations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها حركة ممثل القمر حول مركز العالم على منطقة البروج وقطبيها كل يوم ثلاث دقائق، واثنتي عشرة ثانية، وبها تتحرك جميع أفلاك القمر فينتقل الرأس والذنب، ولذلك ينسب إليهما ويسمى حركة الجوزهر ، ومنها حركة مائل القمر حول مركز العالم على غير معدل النهار، ومنطقة البروج وغير أقطابهما كل يوم، إحدى عشرة درجة، وتسع دقائق، ويتحرك بها الخارج المركز ومركزه ، وتسمى حركة الأوج لظهورها فيه، وقد يسمى مجموع حركة الجوزهر والمائل بحركة الأوج
And among them is the motion of the moon's representative sphere around the center of the world on the ecliptic and its poles each day by three minutes and twelve seconds; by it all the moon's spheres move and the head and tail (nodes) transfer, therefore it is attributed to them and called the motion of al-jawzahr (nodes). And among them is the motion of the moon's inclined sphere around the center of the world not on the equator, the ecliptic, and their poles each day by eleven degrees and nine minutes; by it the eccentric and its center move, and it is called the motion of the apogee because it appears in it. And it may be called the sum of the motion of the nodes and the inclined one the motion of the apogee.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage describes two specific motions of the moon. The first is the motion of the moon's representative sphere (ممثل القمر) which moves around the center of the world on the ecliptic plane at a very slow rate (3 minutes 12 seconds per day). This motion causes all the moon's spheres to move and results in the movement of the nodes (the points where the moon's orbit crosses the ecliptic). This motion is called 'motion of the node' (حركة الجوزهر). The second motion is of the moon's inclined sphere (مائل القمر) which moves not on the equator or ecliptic at a much faster rate (11 degrees 9 minutes per day). This motion moves the eccentric and its center, and is called 'motion of the apogee' (حركة الأوج). Sometimes the combined motion of both is also called 'motion of the apogee'.

Key Terms

حركة الجوزهر
مائل القمر
حركة الأوج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moon has only one motion.
Correction: The moon actually has two distinct motions: the motion of the representative sphere (node motion) and the motion of the inclined sphere (apogee motion).

Checkpoint

B) 11 degrees 9 minutes

Key Takeaway
The moon has two distinct motions: the node motion (3 min 12 sec/day) and the apogee motion (11 deg 9 min/day), each affecting different parts of its orbit.

Daily Motion Rates of the Eccentric Spheres
Numerical values for daily motions of eccentric spheres

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the daily motion values assigned to each planet's eccentric sphere in the Ptolemaic model.

Core Idea
Each planet's eccentric sphere moves at a specific daily rate, from 2 minutes to 24 degrees.

In the Ptolemaic system, each planet's eccentric sphere has a defined daily motion. This passage lists these rates for the sun, planets, and moon, noting that Mercury has a special motion called the director. Understanding these numbers helps grasp the complexity of medieval astronomical models.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to get the overall list of daily motions.
Notice that the passage mentions an exception for Mercury.
2
Identify the motion rates for each body
Go through each celestial body mentioned and note its daily motion value. The sun has the most detailed value.
Values are given in degrees, minutes, seconds, and thirds.
3
Note the special case and location
Mercury has a motion called 'the director' that is excluded from this list. Also observe that these motions are measured on the ecliptic but not its poles.
The passage clarifies that these motions appear at the centers of epicycles.
4
Reflect on the term 'motion of the planet's center'
Consider why this motion is called thus—it affects the center of the epicycle, not the planet itself directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها حركات الأفلاك الخارج المراكز سوى ما يخص باسم المدير لعطارد، وهي في كل يوم للشمس تسع وخمسون دقيقة، وثماني ثوان وعشرون ثالثة، على منطقة البروج دون قطبيها، ولزحل دقيقتان والمشتري خمس دقائق، وللمريخ إحدى وثلاثون دقيقة، وللزهرة (٢) كما للشمس، ولعطارد درجة ونصف، وللقمر أربع وعشرون درجة وثلث وعشرون دقيقة جميع ذلك على غير معدل النهار، ومنطقة البروج، وغير أقطابها، ويظهر في مراكز تداوير الكواكب الستة، ولذلك تسمى حركة مركز الكوكب
And among them are the motions of the eccentric spheres except that which is specifically called the director for Mercury. They are each day: for the sun, 59 minutes, 8 seconds, and 20 thirds, on the ecliptic without its poles; for Saturn, 2 minutes; for Jupiter, 5 minutes; for Mars, 31 minutes; for Venus (2) as for the sun; for Mercury, 1.5 degrees; for the moon, 24 degrees and 23 minutes. All that is not on the equator, the ecliptic, and their poles, and it appears at the centers of the epicycles of the six planets, therefore it is called the motion of the planet's center.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage gives the daily motion rates for the eccentric spheres of the planets, except for Mercury's special one called 'director'. It lists values for the sun, Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Venus, Mercury, and the moon. The motion is not on the standard circles (equator, ecliptic) and appears at the centers of the epicycles, hence called 'motion of the planet's center'.

Motion of Epicycle Halves for Planets and Moon
Motion of epicycle halves for planets and moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific motions of the upper and lower halves of epicycles for the planets and the moon, including their daily rates and direction.

Core Idea
The halves of epicycles move differently—upper for planets, lower for moon—with rates and opposite direction.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explains a key detail in Ptolemaic astronomy: the upper half of the epicycle moves for the five planets, while the lower half moves for the moon. It also provides daily rates and notes that the direction is opposite to the succession (the general eastward motion). Understanding this helps grasp the complex motions of celestial bodies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of motion
Notice that the upper half moves for the five planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Venus, Mercury) on a different plane from their deferent, while the lower half moves for the moon on the deferent and inclined sphere.
The 'upper half' and 'lower half' refer to parts of the epicycle relative to the deferent.
2
Learn the daily rates
For superior planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars), the rate is the excess of the sun's motion over their deferents' motion. For Venus: 37 minutes, Mercury: 3 parts 6 minutes, Moon: 13 parts 4 minutes.
These rates are in degrees and minutes per day.
3
Understand the direction
The passage concludes that the lower halves of the planets' epicycles and the upper half of the moon's epicycle move in the opposite direction of the succession (i.e., east to west).
The succession is the eastward motion of celestial bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها حركة النصف الأعلى من تدوير كل من الخمسة المتحيرة على غير منطقة حاملها، والنصف الأسفل من تدوير القمر على منطقة الحامل والمائل، وهي في كل يوم لتداوير العلوية أعني زحل والمشتري والمريخ فضل حركة الشمس على حركة حواملها ، ولتدوير الزهرة سبعة (۱) وثلاثون دقيقة، ولتدوير عطارد ثلاثة أجزاء وست دقائق، ولتدوير القمر ثلاثة عشر جزءاً وأربع دقائق، ولا محالة يكون النصف الأسفل من تداوير المتحيرة ، والأعلى من تدوير القمر إلى خلاف التوالي.
And among them is the motion of the upper half of the epicycle of each of the five wandering planets on a different plane from its deferent, and the lower half of the moon's epicycle on the plane of the deferent and the inclined sphere. And it is each day: for the epicycles of the superior planets, i.e., Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, [it is] the excess of the sun's motion over the motion of their deferents; for the epicycle of Venus, 37 minutes; for the epicycle of Mercury, 3 parts and 6 minutes; for the epicycle of the moon, 13 parts and 4 minutes. And inevitably the lower half of the epicycles of the wandering planets and the upper half of the moon's epicycle are in the opposite direction of the succession.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage describes the motion of specific halves of epicycles: for the five wandering planets (excluding the sun and moon), the upper half moves in a plane different from its deferent; for the moon, the lower half moves on the deferent and inclined plane. It gives daily rates, and notes that the lower halves of the wandering planets' epicycles and the upper half of the moon's epicycle move in the opposite direction (to the succession).

Key Terms
نصف الأعلى من التدوير
الخمسة المتحيرة
خلاف التوالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: All halves of epicycles move in the same direction.
Correction: The passage states that the lower halves of planets' epicycles and the upper half of the moon's epicycle move opposite to the succession, implying that other halves move in the direction of the succession.

D) Along the ecliptic

Key Takeaway

Latitude of the Moon and Planets
Sanusi explains the moon's latitude and defines planetary latitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the moon and planets acquire latitude from different sources and how latitude is defined in astronomical terms.

Core Idea
Planetary latitude comes from two sources: the inclined deferent and the epicycle.

Al-Sanusi's text discusses the astronomical concept of latitude for the moon and planets. While the moon's latitude arises only from the inclined sphere, the wandering planets have an additional component from the epicycle. This passage defines latitude precisely and contrasts it with longitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms for deferent, epicycle, and inclined sphere.
The passage begins with a quote from al-Maqasid, followed by commentary.
2
Identify the sources of inclination for the moon
The text states that the moon's epicycle moves on the deferent and inclined sphere, so its inclination from the ecliptic only comes from the inclined sphere.
Contrast this with the planets.
3
Identify the sources for the wandering planets
For planets, their epicycles do not move on their deferents' planes, so they have two inclinations: one from the inclined sphere relative to the ecliptic, and one from the epicycle relative to the inclined sphere.
Both inclinations are called latitude.
4
Understand the definition of latitude
Latitude is defined as an arc of a circle through the ecliptic poles, lying between the ecliptic and a line from the planet's center to the highest sphere. It is opposite to longitude.
Note the contrast: longitude is along the ecliptic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (ويقع للقمر) منطقة ميل عن الحامل عنها وبحسب التدوير عن المائل وكل الميلين يسمى بالعرض].
يعني لما كان حركة تدوير القمر على منطقة الحامل والمائل ، لم يقع له ميل عن منطقة البروج إلا بحسب المائل، ولما كان حركة تدوير المتحيرة على غير مناطق حواملها المائلة عن منطقة البروج ، كان لها ميل بحسب المائل عن منطقة البروج، وبحسب التدوير عن منطقة المائل، وكل من الميلين يسمى عرض الكوكب، وحقيقته قوس من الدائرة المارة بقطبي البروج، وبرأس الخط المار بمركز (۲) الكوكب الخارج من مركز العالم إلى الفلك الأعلى (۳) ، يقع بين المنطقة وبين رأس ذلك الخط وهو في مقابلة الطول، وهو قوس (٤) من منطقة البروج على التوالي
[It was said: (And for the moon) there is a plane of inclination from its deferent, and according to the epicycle from the inclined [sphere], and each of the two inclinations is called 'latitude'.]
Meaning: Since the motion of the moon's epicycle is on the plane of the deferent and the inclined sphere, it does not have an inclination from the ecliptic except according to the inclined sphere. And since the motion of the epicycles of the wandering planets is not on the planes of their deferents inclined from the ecliptic, they have an inclination according to the inclined sphere from the ecliptic, and according to the epicycle from the inclined sphere. Each of the two inclinations is called the planet's latitude. Its reality is an arc of the circle passing through the poles of the ecliptic, and the head of the line passing through the center of the planet from the center of the world to the highest sphere, lying between the ecliptic and the head of that line, and it is opposite to longitude, and it is an arc of the ecliptic to the succession.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage begins with a quote from the original text (al-Maqāṣid) stating that the moon has a plane of inclination from its deferent and also from the epicycle, and both inclinations are called 'latitude'. Then the commentary explains: for the moon, because its epicycle moves on the plane of the deferent and the inclined sphere, it only gets inclination from the ecliptic due to the inclined sphere. But for the wandering planets, their epicycles do not move on the planes of their deferents (which themselves are inclined), so they have inclination both from the inclined sphere (relative to the ecliptic) and from the epicycle (relative to the inclined sphere). Both are called the planet's latitude. The reality of latitude is then defined as an arc on a circle through the ecliptic poles and a line through the planet's center from the world center to the highest sphere. This arc lies between the ecliptic and that line, and it is opposite to longitude (which is an arc along the ecliptic).

Key Terms
ميل
عرض الكوكب
طول
منطقة البروج
حامل
تدوير
مائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all planets have the same type of latitude.
Correction: The moon's latitude comes only from the inclined deferent, while planets have an additional component from the epicycle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the moon's latitude arise?
A) Only from the inclined sphere
B) Only from the epicycle
C) From both equally
D) From the deferent alone

Key Takeaway
The moon's latitude arises only from the inclined deferent, while planets have a second component from the epicycle; latitude is an arc through the ecliptic poles opposite to longitude.

Daily Motions of the Planetary Epicycles
Classification of daily epicycle motions for six planets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the daily motions of the epicycles for the six planets and their specific rates and directions.

Core Idea
Each planet's epicycle has a unique daily motion based on its deferent and the sun.

In this passage from the astronomical tradition, the author details the daily motions of the epicycles for the six planets—the five wandering planets and the moon. These motions are not uniform but depend on the planet's position relative to the sun and its deferent. Understanding these rates and directions is key to grasping the Ptolemaic model of planetary motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its structure. Then read the translation. Note that the passage begins with a general statement about the motion appearing in the centers of epicycles.
2
Identify the two types of motion
The passage distinguishes between the motion of the upper half of the epicycle for wandering planets and the lower half for the moon. This direction is important for the model.
The upper half moves opposite to the order of signs for wandering planets, while the moon's lower half does so.
3
Note the daily rates
The passage gives specific daily rates for each planet: for superior planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars) it is the excess of the sun's motion over the deferent motion; for Venus 37 minutes; for Mercury 3 degrees 6 minutes; for the moon 13 degrees 4 minutes.
These rates are given in degrees and minutes per day.
4
Understand the direction
The final sentence states that the lower half of the wandering planets' epicycles and the upper half of the moon's epicycle move opposite to the order of the signs (i.e., retrograde direction).
This is a key detail for the overall motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
درجة وثلث وعشرون دقيقة جميع ذلك على غير معدل النهار، ومنطقة البروج، وغير أقطابها، ويظهر في مراكز تداوير الكواكب الستة، ولذلك تسمى حركة مركز الكوكب ، ومنها حركة النصف الأعلى من تدوير كل من الخمسة المتحيرة على غير منطقة حاملها، والنصف الأسفل من تدوير القمر على منطقة الحامل والمائل، وهي في كل يوم لتداوير العلوية أعني زحل والمشتري والمريخ فضل حركة الشمس على حركة حواملها ، ولتدوير الزهرة سبعة وثلاثون دقيقة، ولتدوير عطارد ثلاثة أجزاء وست دقائق، ولتدوير القمر ثلاثة عشر جزءاً وأربع دقائق، ولا محالة يكون النصف الأسفل من تداوير المتحيرة ، والأعلى من تدوير القمر إلى خلاف التوالي.
A degree and twenty-three minutes, all of that relative to the celestial equator and the ecliptic and their poles, and it appears in the centers of the epicycles of the six planets, and therefore it is called the motion of the center of the planet. And among them is the motion of the upper half of the epicycle of each of the five wandering planets relative to the plane of its deferent, and the lower half of the epicycle of the moon relative to the plane of the deferent and the inclined plane. And it is each day for the epicycles of the superior ones, namely Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars, the excess of the motion of the sun over the motion of their deferents. And for the epicycle of Venus, thirty-seven minutes. And for the epicycle of Mercury, three degrees and six minutes. And for the epicycle of the moon, thirteen degrees and four minutes. And necessarily, the lower half of the epicycles of the wandering planets and the upper half of the epicycle of the moon are opposite to the order of the signs.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the daily motions of the epicycles of the six planets (the five wandering planets plus the moon). It begins by stating that the motion of the center of the planet appears in the centers of the epicycles, and it is measured relative to the celestial equator, the ecliptic, and their poles. Then it describes two types of motion: the motion of the upper half of the epicycle for each wandering planet (relative to its deferent plane) and the motion of the lower half of the epicycle for the moon (relative to its deferent and inclined plane). The passage then specifies the daily rates: for the superior planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars), the epicycle's daily motion equals the excess of the sun's motion over the motion of their deferents; for Venus (37 minutes per day), for Mercury (3 degrees 6 minutes), and for the moon (13 degrees 4 minutes). Finally, it notes that the lower half of the epicycles of the wandering planets and the upper half of the moon's epicycle move opposite to the order of the zodiacal signs.

The Moon's Inclination and Planetary Width
Defines the moon's inclination and the concept of a planet's width (ard) from the ecliptic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the moon and wandering planets produce inclination (width) from the ecliptic, and how width is defined geometrically.

Core Idea
A planet's width is the celestial latitude, defined by its inclination from the ecliptic.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explains a key concept in Ptolemaic astronomy: the width (ard) of a planet, which is its celestial latitude relative to the ecliptic. The author distinguishes between the moon, which has only one source of inclination, and the wandering planets, which have two. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how medieval Islamic astronomers modeled planetary motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the technical terms like 'milan' (inclination) and 'ard' (width).
Don't worry about fully understanding the geometry yet.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage describes two cases: the moon and the wandering planets. Find how each produces inclination. The moon has only one (from the inclined plane), while the wandering planets have two (from the inclined plane and from the epicycle).
3
Understand the definition of width
Read the definition of width (ard) as an arc on a circle through the ecliptic poles. Note how it contrasts with longitude. This is a geometric definition.
Width is the celestial latitude, longitude is the celestial longitude.
4
Relate to the commentary
Read the provided commentary to solidify your understanding. It simplifies the geometric description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (ويقع للقمر) منطقة ميل عن الحامل عنها وبحسب التدوير عن المائل وكل الميلين يسمى بالعرض]. البروج بحسب المائل فقط، والمتحيرة بحسب المائل، أعني يعني لما كان حركة تدوير القمر على منطقة الحامل والمائل ، لم يقع له ميل عن منطقة البروج إلا بحسب المائل، ولما كان حركة تدوير المتحيرة على غير مناطق حواملها المائلة عن منطقة البروج ، كان لها ميل بحسب المائل عن منطقة البروج، وبحسب التدوير عن منطقة المائل، وكل من الميلين يسمى عرض الكوكب، وحقيقته قوس من الدائرة المارة بقطبي البروج، وبرأس الخط المار بمركز الكوكب الخارج من مركز العالم إلى الفلك الأعلى ، يقع بين المنطقة وبين رأس ذلك الخط وهو في مقابلة الطول، وهو قوس من منطقة البروج على التوالي، يقع به نقطة الاعتدال الربيعي، وبين الكوكب إن كان عديم العرض، أو بين التي تقطع دائرة عرضه فلك البروج عليها إن كان ذا عرض .
[He said: 'And for the moon there occurs an inclination from the deferent according to the inclined plane, and according to the epicycle from the inclined plane, and both inclinations are called the width.'] Only according to the inclined plane for the ecliptic, and for the wandering planets according to the inclined plane, meaning: because the motion of the moon's epicycle is on the plane of the deferent and the inclined plane, it does not have an inclination from the ecliptic except according to the inclined plane. And because the motion of the epicycles of the wandering planets is not on the planes of their deferents inclined from the ecliptic, they have an inclination according to the inclined plane from the ecliptic, and according to the epicycle from the plane of the inclined. And each of the two inclinations is called the width of the planet. And its reality is an arc of the circle passing through the poles of the ecliptic, and at the head of the line passing through the center of the planet from the center of the world to the outermost sphere, lying between the ecliptic and the head of that line, and it is opposite to the longitude. And it is an arc of the ecliptic successively, lying at the vernal equinox point, and between the planet if it has no width, or between that which cuts its circle of width upon the ecliptic if it has width.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of a planet's width (ard, celestial latitude). It begins with a quotation stating that the moon has two types of inclination: one from its deferent according to the inclined plane, and another from its epicycle according to the inclined plane, both called width. The author then explains that the moon's epicycle moves on the plane of its deferent and the inclined plane, so its inclination from the ecliptic is only according to the inclined plane. For the wandering planets, their epicycles move on planes not on the ecliptic but inclined, so they have two inclinations: one from the ecliptic due to the inclined plane of their deferents, and another from the moving inclined plane due to the epicycle. Both are called the width of the planet. The reality of width is then defined as an arc on a circle through the ecliptic poles and the line from the planet's center outwards, lying between the ecliptic and that line, opposite to longitude. It is measured along the ecliptic beginning at the vernal equinox, ending at the planet if it has no width, or at the intersection of the planet's circle of width with the ecliptic if it has width.

Key Terms
الميل
العرض
المائل
التدوير
الحامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing width (latitude) with longitude or thinking width is a physical distance rather than an angular measure.
Correction: Width is an angular arc (celestial latitude) defined geometrically on a circle through the ecliptic poles, measured from the ecliptic to the planet's position.

Checkpoint
Why does the moon have only one type of inclination from the ecliptic?
A) Because its epicycle moves on the plane of the deferent and the inclined plane
B) Because it has no epicycle
C) Because its deferent is not inclined
D) Because it has two deferents

Key Takeaway
A planet's width (ard) is its celestial latitude, defined as an arc between the ecliptic and the planet's position along a great circle through the ecliptic poles.

The Two Nodes and the Parts of the Spheres
Defines the two nodes and states the number of sphere parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the two nodes (al-jauzahran) and how they lead to the division of the spheres into twenty-four parts.

Core Idea
The nodes mark where planets cross the ecliptic, dividing the celestial spheres into twenty-four parts.

In this passage, the author introduces the two nodes of the inclined orbit—the points where a planet's path intersects the ecliptic. These nodes, called the head and tail, are fundamental to understanding the structure of the spheres. Let's explore how this leads to the conclusion that the parts of the spheres are twenty-four.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the nodes
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It begins by stating the topic of the two intersection points and then defines the head and tail.
Notice the quotation marks; the author is citing an earlier text.
2
Identify the head and tail
Find where the passage defines which node is called the head and which is the tail. Understand that the head is where the planet crosses northward.
3
Understand the twenty-four parts
The passage states that from this, it becomes apparent that the parts of the spheres are twenty-four. Reflect on how the nodes and the inclined planes lead to this enumeration.
This connects to the broader cosmology of the time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحديث عن نقطتي تقاطع المائل [قال ( ونقطتان تقاطع المائل والممثل بالجوزهرين والتي تجاوزها الكواكب إلى الشمال بالرأس والأخرى بالذنب، فظهر أن جزئيات الأفلاك أربعة وعشرون، وكذا بسائط الحركات، ومبناه على اعتقاد الانتظام في السموات مع مشاهدة الكثير من الاختلافات مثل السرعة، والبطء بعد التوسط والرجعة والوقوف بعد الاستقامة ولا شك أن من الخارج المركز الذي يحرك الشمس مثلاً حركة متشابهة حول مركزة القوس الذي تكون في النصف الأوجي من الممثل، أعظم من التي تكون في النصف الحضيضي وأنها لا تقطع كل نصف إلا بقطع ما فيه من الخارج، فيكون زمان قطع النصف الأوجي أكثر ، فترى الحركة أبطأ، فلهذا كانت مدة الربيع والصيف أكثر مدة من الخريف والشتاء، وأن الكواكب إذا كان من التدوير في النصف الذي يوافق حركته حركة الحامل، يرى سريعاً، وإذا كان له في النصف الآخر، فإن كانت حركته أقل من حركة الحامل يرى بطيئاً، وإن انتهت إلى التساوي. وذلك في المتحيرة لا غير، يرى واقفاً وإن زادت يرى راجعاً]. والممثل بالجوزهرين يعني بالمائل مائل القمر والأفلاك الخارجة المراكز للخمس المتحيرة لما عرفت من أنها ليست في سطح منطقة البروج [بل مائلة عنها، فلهذا تقاطع الأفلاك الممثلة . وسموا من الجوزهرين النقطة التي يجاوزها الكوكب إلى شمال منطقة البروج ] بالرأس، والتي يقابلها بالمذنب، تشبيهاً للمشكل الحادث بين نصفي المنطقتين من الجانب الأقل بالتنين، وجعل الأخذ في
Discussion of the two points of intersection of the inclined plane. [He said: 'And two points of intersection of the inclined and the similar, called the two nodes [al-jauzahran], and which the planets cross to the north is called the head, and the other the tail. So it became apparent that the parts of the spheres are twenty-four, and likewise the simple motions. And it is based on the belief in regularity in the heavens despite observing many irregularities such as speed, slowness after medium, retrograde, and stationarity after direct motion. And there is no doubt that from the eccentric which moves the sun, for example, with a uniform motion around its center, the arc that is in the apogee half of the similar is greater than that which is in the perigee half, and it does not traverse each half except by traversing what is in it of the eccentric. So the time of traversing the apogee half is longer, so you see the motion slower. Therefore the duration of spring and summer is longer than that of autumn and winter. And that the planets, if they are in the epicycle in the half whose motion agrees with the motion of the deferent, are seen fast, and if they are in the other half, if their motion is less than the motion of the deferent, they are seen slow, and if it equals it—and that is only in the wandering planets—they are seen stationary, and if it exceeds, they are seen retrograde.' And the similar [called] the nodes means by the inclined plane the inclination of the moon and the eccentric spheres for the five wandering planets, because you know that they are not in the plane of the ecliptic [but inclined from it. Therefore the similar spheres intersect. And they call from the nodes the point which the planet crosses to the north of the ecliptic] the head, and the opposite the tail, likening the shape formed between the two halves of the two planes from the lesser side to a dragon. And they made the taking in...
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage discusses the two nodes (al-jauzahran) of the inclined orbit and explains how the eccentric and epicyclic models account for observed irregularities in planetary motion. It begins by stating the topic: the two intersection points of the inclined plane with the ecliptic. A quotation then defines the nodes: the point where a planet crosses northward is the head (ra's), the opposite is the tail (dhanab). From this, it concludes that the parts of the spheres are twenty-four, as are the simple motions, based on the principle of regularity despite observed variations. The quotation then explains that the eccentric model for the sun produces unequal arc lengths on the epicycle (apogee vs. perigee), causing slower motion in the apogee half, which accounts for the longer durations of spring and summer. For the planets, their epicycles cause apparent speed, slowness, stationarity, and retrograde depending on whether the epicyclic motion agrees with or opposes the deferent motion. After the quotation, the author clarifies that 'the similar' referred to in the nodes terminology means the inclined plane for the moon and the eccentric spheres for the five wandering planets, which are inclined to the ecliptic, hence their spheres intersect. The nodes are named head and tail by analogy to a dragon. The passage ends abruptly, possibly continuing the explanation.

Explaining Apparent Motions: Eccentric and Epicycle
Explains apparent speed, stationarity, and retrograde via eccentric and epicycle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eccentric and epicyclic models explain apparent irregularities like speed, stationarity, and retrograde motion.

Core Idea
Uniform motions combined with eccentrics and epicycles produce observed speed changes and retrograde motion.

The passage now turns to explaining how observed irregularities—speed, slowness, stationarity, and retrograde—are consistent with uniform circular motions. Using the eccentric model for the sun and the epicycle model for planets, the author demonstrates that these apparent phenomena arise from geometric combinations. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the sun's eccentric model
Read the explanation of how the eccentric model for the sun causes slower motion in the apogee half, leading to longer seasons.
The apogee half has larger arc on the deferent, so more time is needed.
2
Analyze the epicycle effect for planets
Focus on the condition described: when the epicycle moves in the same direction as the deferent, the planet appears fast; in opposite direction, it appears slow.
The key is the relative motion of the epicycle and deferent.
3
Identify conditions for stationarity and retrograde
When the epicyclic motion equals the deferent's motion, the planet appears stationary; when it exceeds, it appears retrograde.
This only occurs for the wandering planets (Mercury, Venus, Mars, Jupiter, Saturn).

Will you today make a concrete commitment to realign your focus with the unseen reality of His plan?
