Compounds Without Temperament: The First Type – Atmospheric Phenomena
Al-Taftazani describes the first type of compounds without temperament: atmospheric phenomena from vapor and smoke.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic scholars like al-Taftazani explained atmospheric phenomena such as rain, snow, hail, fog, thunder, lightning, and shooting stars using the concepts of vapor and smoke within an Aristotelian framework.

Core Idea
Atmospheric phenomena arise from vapor and smoke interacting with heat and cold.

Al-Taftazani now turns to the third category of bodily substances: compounds without temperament. These are not living beings but phenomena formed in the atmosphere. The first type includes everything that occurs above the earth, from rain and snow to thunder and shooting stars. This passage presents a detailed account based on Aristotelian meteorology, where vapor and smoke are the two active materials.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening classification
The author introduces three types of compounds without temperament, but only the first type is discussed here. Notice the phrase 'القسم الثالث' and 'النوع الأول'.
The passage focuses entirely on the first type: what occurs above the earth.
2
Identify the two materials: vapor and smoke
Vapor (بخار) and smoke (دخان) are the two basic materials that give rise to all the phenomena described. Vapor is moist, smoke is dry and fiery.
3
Trace the journey of vapor
Follow how vapor either becomes air when rarefied, or reaches the frigid zone to form clouds and precipitation, or stays low to become fog, hoarfrost, or dew.
Note the role of cold (البرد) in condensation and freezing.
4
Understand the role of smoke
Smoke trapped in clouds causes thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts. Thick smoke reaching the sphere of fire creates shooting stars and other fiery phenomena.
These are physical explanations within the Aristotelian paradigm.
5
Consider the broader context
Reflect that this passage shows a naturalistic approach to explaining weather and celestial events, grounding them in observable processes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (القسم الثالث) في المركبات التي لا مزاج لها وهي أنواع : النوع الأول : ما يحدث فوق الأرض البخار المتصاعد قد يلطف فيصبح هواء، وقد يبلغ الطبقة الزمهريرية فيتكاثف فيجتمع سحاباً ويتقاطر مطراً، وربما يصيبه برد قبل تشكله قطرات فينزل ثلجاً، أو بعده فبرداً، وقد لا يبلغ فيصير ضباباً إن كثر، ونزل صقيعاً، أو طلاً إن قل وتكاثف برد الليل، وربما ينعقد البخار الكثير سحاباً ماطراً بتكاثفه بالبرد، وأن يتصعد إلى الزمهريرية لمانع، وقد يتصاعد مع البخار دخان، فيحتبس في السحاب فيمزقه بعنف إلى فوق إن بقي على حرارته، وإلى تحت إن أصابه برد، فيحدث من تمزيقه ومصاكته إياه صوت هو الرعد، ونارية لطيفة هي البرق، أو كثيفة هي الصاعقة، وقد يشتعل الدخان الغليظ بالوصول إلى كرة النار، فيرى كأنه كوكب انقض، وهو الشهاب، وقد يدوم احتراقه، فيقع على صورة ذؤابة، أو ذنب، أو حية، أو حيوان له قرون، فيدور بدوران الفلك، وقد ينزل اشتعاله إلى الأرض وهو الحريق.
He said: The third section: On compounds that have no temperament, and they are types: The first type: what occurs above the earth: rising vapor may become rarefied and become air, or may reach the frigid zone and become dense, gathering as clouds and dripping as rain, and perhaps cold affects it before it forms droplets so it comes down as snow, or after that so it comes down as hail; or it may not reach (the frigid zone) and becomes fog if abundant, and comes down as hoarfrost, or dew if little and condensed by night cold; and perhaps abundant vapor may become clouds that rain by condensation with cold, or (the vapor) may rise to the frigid zone due to an obstacle; and smoke may rise with the vapor, then be trapped in the cloud, tearing it violently upward if it retains its heat, or downward if cold affects it; and from its tearing and friction with it arises a sound which is thunder, and a subtle fire which is lightning, or a dense fire which is lightning bolt; and thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, and appears as though a meteor has fallen, and it is the shooting star; and its combustion may persist, and it falls in the form of a tail, a dragon, or a snake, or an animal with horns, revolving with the revolution of the orb; and its conflagration may descend to the earth, and it is the fire.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is from the matn (original text) of Al-Maqasid. It presents the third section on compounds without temperament (al-murakkabat allati la mizaj laha). The author divides them into three types based on location, but here he only discusses the first type: what occurs above the earth in the atmosphere. He describes the processes of vapor (bukhar) and smoke (dukhan). Vapor, when rarefied, becomes air; when it reaches the cold (zamhariri) zone, it condenses into clouds and falls as rain. If it freezes before forming drops, it becomes snow; after forming drops, it becomes hail. If it does not reach the cold zone, it becomes fog, hoarfrost, or dew. Smoke trapped in clouds causes thunder (by tearing the cloud) and lightning (subtle fire) or thunderbolts (dense fire). Thick smoke that reaches the sphere of fire creates shooting stars and other fiery phenomena. The description follows Aristotelian meteorology.

مزاج (temperament)
بخار (vapor)
دخان (smoke)
سحاب (cloud)
مطر (rain)
ثلج (snow)
برد (hail)
ضباب (fog)
صقيع (hoarfrost)
طل (dew)
رعد (thunder)
برق (lightning)
صاعقة (thunderbolt)
شهاب (shooting star)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These explanations are merely symbolic or allegorical.
Correction: Al-Taftazani presents these as genuine physical processes within the Aristotelian natural philosophy of his time.

Checkpoint

A) Collision of clouds
B) Tearing of clouds by trapped smoke

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani explains weather and celestial phenomena as natural processes of vapor and smoke, not as direct divine acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a thunderstorm, do you pause to consider the intricate natural processes described here, or do you simply see it as a random event? How might recognizing the order in creation deepen your appreciation for Allah's wisdom?
?
The passage explains these phenomena through vapor and smoke without mentioning direct divine intervention at each step. Does this challenge your view of how Allah interacts with the world, or does it strengthen your trust in the natural laws He established?
?
Are you comfortable with the idea that some natural events have secondary causes, or do you feel it diminishes God's power? How does this lesson affect your understanding of tawakkul—relying on Allah while acknowledging the means He placed?

Methodology: From Simples to Compounds
Sanusi explains his methodology and classification of compounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's rationale for ordering the study of compounds after simples and the classification of compounds based on location.

Core Idea
The author prioritizes compounds without temperament because they are simpler and closer to simples.

Before diving into the detailed causes of atmospheric phenomena, the commentary explains why the author arranged the topics as he did and how he classified compounds into three types based on where they occur. This methodological foundation helps us appreciate the systematic approach of Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the methodology passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the author's ordering and classification. Notice how he justifies starting with compounds without temperament.
2
Identify the three types
The passage says there are three types based on location: above earth (air), on earth's surface, and in earth. Find this classification.
Only the first type is discussed in detail later.
3
Reflect on the rationale
Why does the author say compounds without temperament are 'more similar to simples'? Think about the lack of firm composition and limited elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد الفراغ من مباحث البسائط بقسميها أعني الفلكية والعنصرية، شرع في قسمي مباحث المركبات أعني التي لا مزاج لها والتي لها مزاج، وقدم ذلك لكونه أشبه بالبسائط من جهة عدم استحكام تركيبه ومن جهة جواز اقتصاره على عنصرين أو ثلاثة، وجعله ثلاثة أنواع، لأن حدوثه إما فوق الأرض أعني في الهواء، وإما على وجه الأرض وإما في الأرض فالنوع الأول منه ما يتكون من البخار، ومنه ما يتكون من الدخان وكلاهما بالحرارة فإنها تحلل من الرطب أجزاء هوائية ومائية هي البخار، ومن اليابس أجزاء أرضية تخالطها أجزاء نارية، وقلما تخلو عن هوائية وهي الدخان فالبخار المتصاعد قد يلطف بتحليل الحرارة أجزاؤه المائية، فيصير هواء، وقد يبلغ الطبقة الزمهريرية فيتكاثف فيجتمع سحاباً ويتقاطر مطراً إن لم يكن البرد شديداً، وإن أصابه برد شديد فيجمد السحاب قبل تشكله بشكل القطرات نزل ثلجاً، أو بعد تشكله بذلك نزل برداً صغيراً مستديراً إن كان من سحاب بعيد لذوبان الزوايا بالحركة والاحتكاك، وإلا فكبيراً غير مستدير في الغالب، وإنما يكون البرد في هواء ربيعي أو خريفي لفرط التحليل في الصيفي والجمود في الشتوي، وقد لا يبلغ البخار المتصاعد الطبقة الزمهريرية، فإن كثر صار ضباباً، وإن قل وتكاثف ببرد الليل نزل صقيعاً أو طلاً، وقد يتصاعد مع البخار دخان، فيحتبس في السحاب فيمزقه بعنف إلى فوق إن بقي على حرارته، وإلى تحت إن أصابه البرد، فيحدث من تمزيقه ومصاكته إياه صوت هو الرعد، ونارية لطيفة هي البرق، أو كثيفة هي الصاعقة، وقد يشتعل الدخان الغليظ بالوصول إلى كرة النار، فيرى كأنه كوكب انقض، وهو الشهاب، وقد يدوم اشتعاله فيقع على صورة ذؤابة، أو ذنب، أو حية، أو حيوان له قرون، فيدور بدوران الفلك، وقد ينزل اشتعاله إلى الأرض وهو الحريق.
After finishing the discussion of simples with their two types, namely the celestial and the elemental, he began the two types of discussions of compounds, namely those without temperament and those with temperament, and he prioritized that because it is more similar to simples in terms of the lack of firmness of its composition and in terms of the permissibility of its being limited to two or three elements, and he made it three types, because its occurrence is either above the earth, meaning in the air, or on the face of the earth, or in the earth. So the first type includes what is composed from vapor, and includes what is composed from smoke, and both are by heat, for it decomposes from the moist parts that are aerial and watery, which are vapor, and from the dry parts that are earthy mixed with fiery parts, and rarely are they free from aerial parts, and that is smoke. So the rising vapor may become rarefied by the analysis of its watery parts by heat, thus becoming air, or it may reach the frigid zone and become dense, gathering as clouds and dripping as rain if the cold is not severe; and if severe cold afflicts it, it freezes the cloud before it forms into droplets and it falls as snow; or after its formation into droplets, it falls as small round hail if it is from a distant cloud due to the melting of angles by motion and friction; otherwise, it is large and mostly not round. And hail occurs only in spring or autumn air because of excessive analysis in summer and freezing in winter. And if the rising vapor does not reach the frigid zone, then if abundant it becomes fog, and if little and condensed by night cold, it falls as hoarfrost or dew. And smoke may rise with the vapor, then be trapped in the cloud, tearing it violently upward if it retains its heat, or downward if cold afflicts it; then from its tearing and friction with it arises a sound which is thunder, and a subtle fire which is lightning, or a dense fire which is the thunderbolt. And thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, and appears as though a meteor has fallen, and it is the shooting star; and its combustion may persist, and it falls in the form of a tail, or a dragon, or a snake, or an animal with horns, revolving with the revolution of the orb; and its conflagration may descend to the earth, and it is the fire.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is the commentary (sharh) on the matn. The author explains his ordering: after discussing simples (celestial and elemental), he moves to compounds, prioritizing those without temperament because they are simpler and closer to simples. He reiterates the three types based on location and then provides a more detailed causal account of the first type. He distinguishes between vapor (from moist parts decomposed by heat) and smoke (from dry parts with fire). He describes the processes of rarefaction, condensation, freezing, and combustion. He adds new details, such as why hail is small and round if from a distant cloud, and why hail occurs in spring/autumn. He also repeats the phenomena of thunder, lightning, thunderbolts, and shooting stars with slightly more detail. The commentary reinforces the naturalistic explanations of the matn.

Key Terms
بسائط (simples)
فلكية (celestial)
عنصرية (elemental)
مركبات (compounds)
مزاج (temperament)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are more important
B) Because they are more similar to simples
C) Because they are easier to study
D) Because they are more numerous

Key Takeaway
The author's classification of compounds into three types (atmospheric, surface, subterranean) is based on their location, and he discusses atmospheric phenomena first because they are simplest.

Phenomena from Vapor: From Vapor to Rain, Snow, and Hail
Sanusi explains the transformation of vapor into various atmospheric phenomena.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rising vapor is transformed by heat and cold into various atmospheric phenomena such as rain, snow, hail, fog, hoarfrost, and dew.

Core Idea
Vapor, when heated, rises and can either become air or condense into clouds, rain, snow, or hail depending on cold conditions.

The commentary now details the transformations of vapor, which is composed of aerial and watery parts. Depending on whether it reaches the frigid zone and the intensity of cold, vapor can turn into air, clouds, rain, snow, hail, fog, hoarfrost, or dew. This lesson traces the causal chain from vapor to each phenomenon.

Guided Path
1
Read the vapor passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'فالنوع الأول منه ما يتكون من البخار' down to 'نزل صقيعاً أو طلاً'. Notice the branching conditions.
The passage describes a decision tree based on temperature and altitude.
2
Map the transformations
Create a mental map: vapor -> rarefied to air; or vapor reaches frigid zone -> condenses to cloud -> if not severe cold -> rain; if severe cold -> snow (if before droplet formation) or hail (if after).
3
Identify rare phenomena
The passage also describes fog, hoarfrost, and dew when vapor does not reach the frigid zone. Note the conditions: abundant or little, and condensation by night cold.

Source Text

After finishing the discussion of simples with their two types, namely the celestial and the elemental, he began the two types of discussions of compounds, namely those without temperament and those with temperament, and he prioritized that because it is more similar to simples in terms of the lack of firmness of its composition and in terms of the permissibility of its being limited to two or three elements, and he made it three types, because its occurrence is either above the earth, meaning in the air, or on the face of the earth, or in the earth. ?

?

Phenomena from Smoke: Thunder, Lightning, and Shooting Stars
Sanusi explains the atmospheric phenomena caused by smoke.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how smoke, when trapped in clouds or rising to the sphere of fire, produces thunder, lightning, thunderbolts, shooting stars, and other fiery phenomena.

Core Idea
Smoke mixing with vapor in clouds causes violent motions that generate sound (thunder) and fire (lightning, thunderbolts), and smoke reaching the sphere of fire produces shooting stars and other fiery apparitions.

Having covered vapor, the commentary now turns to smoke, which arises from dry parts. When smoke rises with vapor and becomes trapped in a cloud, it can tear the cloud, producing thunder, lightning, or thunderbolts. If thick smoke reaches the sphere of fire, it ignites and appears as shooting stars, sometimes with tails or shapes that revolve with the celestial sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the smoke passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'وقد يتصاعد مع البخار دخان' to the end. Note the two scenarios: smoke trapped in cloud, and smoke reaching the sphere of fire.
2
Understand how thunder and lightning are produced
The passage says smoke tears the cloud violently, causing a sound (thunder) and a subtle fire (lightning). If the fire is dense, it is a thunderbolt.
3
Understand shooting stars and other fiery forms
Thick smoke reaching the sphere of fire ignites and appears as a shooting star. If combustion persists, it can take various shapes (tail, dragon, etc.) and revolve with the orb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد الفراغ من مباحث البسائط بقسميها أعني الفلكية والعنصرية، شرع في قسمي مباحث المركبات أعني التي لا مزاج لها والتي لها مزاج، وقدم ذلك لكونه أشبه بالبسائط من جهة عدم استحكام تركيبه ومن جهة جواز اقتصاره على عنصرين أو ثلاثة، وجعله ثلاثة أنواع، لأن حدوثه إما فوق الأرض أعني في الهواء، وإما على وجه الأرض وإما في الأرض فالنوع الأول منه ما يتكون من البخار، ومنه ما يتكون من الدخان وكلاهما بالحرارة فإنها تحلل من الرطب أجزاء هوائية ومائية هي البخار، ومن اليابس أجزاء أرضية تخالطها أجزاء نارية، وقلما تخلو عن هوائية وهي الدخان فالبخار المتصاعد قد يلطف بتحليل الحرارة أجزاؤه المائية، فيصير هواء، وقد يبلغ الطبقة الزمهريرية فيتكاثف فيجتمع سحاباً ويتقاطر مطراً إن لم يكن البرد شديداً، وإن أصابه برد شديد فيجمد السحاب قبل تشكله بشكل القطرات نزل ثلجاً، أو بعد تشكله بذلك نزل برداً صغيراً مستديراً إن كان من سحاب بعيد لذوبان الزوايا بالحركة والاحتكاك، وإلا فكبيراً غير مستدير في الغالب، وإنما يكون البرد في هواء ربيعي أو خريفي لفرط التحليل في الصيفي والجمود في الشتوي، وقد لا يبلغ البخار المتصاعد الطبقة الزمهريرية، فإن كثر صار ضباباً، وإن قل وتكاثف ببرد الليل نزل صقيعاً أو طلاً، وقد يتصاعد مع البخار دخان، فيحتبس في السحاب فيمزقه بعنف إلى فوق إن بقي على حرارته، وإلى تحت إن أصابه البرد، فيحدث من تمزيقه ومصاكته إياه صوت هو الرعد، ونارية لطيفة هي البرق، أو كثيفة هي الصاعقة، وقد يشتعل الدخان الغليظ بالوصول إلى كرة النار، فيرى كأنه كوكب انقض، وهو الشهاب، وقد يدوم اشتعاله فيقع على صورة ذؤابة، أو ذنب، أو حية، أو حيوان له قرون، فيدور بدوران الفلك، وقد ينزل اشتعاله إلى الأرض وهو الحريق.
After finishing the discussion of simples with their two types, namely the celestial and the elemental, he began the two types of discussions of compounds, namely those without temperament and those with temperament, and he prioritized that because it is more similar to simples in terms of the lack of firmness of its composition and in terms of the permissibility of its being limited to two or three elements, and he made it three types, because its occurrence is either above the earth, meaning in the air, or on the face of the earth, or in the earth. So the first type includes what is composed from vapor, and includes what is composed from smoke, and both are by heat, for it decomposes from the moist parts that are aerial and watery, which are vapor, and from the dry parts that are earthy mixed with fiery parts, and rarely are they free from aerial parts, and that is smoke. So the rising vapor may become rarefied by the analysis of its watery parts by heat, thus becoming air, or it may reach the frigid zone and become dense, gathering as clouds and dripping as rain if the cold is not severe; and if severe cold afflicts it, it freezes the cloud before it forms into droplets and it falls as snow; or after its formation into droplets, it falls as small round hail if it is from a distant cloud due to the melting of angles by motion and friction; otherwise, it is large and mostly not round. And hail occurs only in spring or autumn air because of excessive analysis in summer and freezing in winter. And if the rising vapor does not reach the frigid zone, then if abundant it becomes fog, and if little and condensed by night cold, it falls as hoarfrost or dew. And smoke may rise with the vapor, then be trapped in the cloud, tearing it violently upward if it retains its heat, or downward if cold afflicts it; then from its tearing and friction with it arises a sound which is thunder, and a subtle fire which is lightning, or a dense fire which is the thunderbolt. And thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, and appears as though a meteor has fallen, and it is the shooting star; and its combustion may persist, and it falls in the form of a tail, or a dragon, or a snake, or an animal with horns, revolving with the revolution of the orb; and its conflagration may descend to the earth, and it is the fire.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is the commentary (sharh) on the matn. The author explains his ordering: after discussing simples (celestial and elemental), he moves to compounds, prioritizing those without temperament because they are simpler and closer to simples. He reiterates the three types based on location and then provides a more detailed causal account of the first type. He distinguishes between vapor (from moist parts decomposed by heat) and smoke (from dry parts with fire). He describes the processes of rarefaction, condensation, freezing, and combustion. He adds new details, such as why hail is small and round if from a distant cloud, and why hail occurs in spring/autumn. He also repeats the phenomena of thunder, lightning, thunderbolts, and shooting stars with slightly more detail. The commentary reinforces the naturalistic explanations of the matn.

Key Terms
دخان (smoke)
سحاب (cloud)
رعد (thunder)
برق (lightning)
صاعقة (thunderbolt)
شهاب (shooting star)
حريق (fire)
كرة النار (sphere of fire)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think thunder is caused by clouds colliding, but the commentary says it is caused by smoke tearing the cloud.
Correction: The passage explicitly attributes thunder to the tearing and friction of smoke within the cloud.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the cause of thunder?
A) Collision of clouds
B) Friction of smoke tearing the cloud
C) Ignition of smoke
D) Wind in the sky

Key Takeaway
Smoke trapped in clouds causes thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts through violent tearing, while smoke reaching the sphere of fire produces shooting stars and other fiery phenomena.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a thunderstorm, do you ever pause to reflect on the intricate processes described by Sanusi—the smoke trapped in clouds, the violent tearing, the resulting sound and fire? How does seeing lightning as a sign of Allah's design change your state of heart in that moment? Will you make a conscious effort to remember the Creator during the next storm?
?
The passage describes thick smoke reaching the sphere of fire and igniting into shooting stars. What 'smoke' in your life—such as heedlessness or worldly attachments—might be rising and eventually igniting into spiritual consequences if left unchecked? What one step will you take this week to extinguish that smoke before it reaches the fire?

Classification of Compounds and Vapor-Based Weather
Classification of compounds and causal explanation of vapor-based atmospheric phenomena.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author classifies compounds and explains the formation of rain, snow, hail, mist, dew, and frost from vapor.

Core Idea
Vapor, transformed by heat and cold, systematically produces all common weather phenomena.

This passage begins the discussion of compounds by first classifying them and then focusing on atmospheric phenomena. The author explains how vapor, generated by heat acting on moisture, undergoes various transformations in the air, leading to familiar weather events like rain, snow, and hail. Through this naturalistic explanation, the scholar demonstrates the order and wisdom in creation.

Guided Path
1
Follow the classification of compounds
Start by noticing how the author transitions from simple bodies to compounds, dividing them into two types based on mixture. Then note the three locations where compounds occur: above earth, on earth's surface, and in earth.
The first type (above earth) is the focus because it resembles simple bodies.
2
Understand the origin of vapor
Identify that vapor (bukhār) arises from heat dissolving moist things into aerial and watery parts. Heat also produces smoke from dry things, but this lesson focuses on vapor.
3
Trace the outcomes of vapor at different heights
Follow the ascending vapor: it may become subtle air, or reach the frigid zone to form clouds, rain, snow, hail. Below that zone, it may form mist, dew, or frost. Also note obstacles that cause rain without reaching the frigid zone.
Notice the ratios mentioned: frost is to dew as snow is to rain.

بعد الفراغ من مباحث البسائط بقسميها أعني الفلكية والعنصرية، شرع في قسمي مباحث المركبات أعني التي لا مزاج لها والتي لها مزاج، وقدم ذلك لكونه أشبه بالبسائط من جهة عدم استحكام تركيبه ومن جهة جواز اقتصاره على عنصرين أو ثلاثة، وجعله ثلاثة أنواع، لأن حدوثه إما فوق الأرض أعني في الهواء، وإما على وجه الأرض وإما في الأرض فالنوع الأول منه ما يتكون من البخار، ومنه ما يتكون من الدخان وكلاهما بالحرارة فإنها تحلل من الرطب أجزاء هوائية ومائية هي البخار، ومن اليابس أجزاء أرضية تخالطها أجزاء نارية وقلما تخلو عن هوائية وهي الدخان فالبخار المتصاعد قد يلطف بتحليل الحرارة أجزاؤه المائية، فيصير هواء، وقد يبلغ الطبقة الزمهريرية فيتكاثف فيجتمع سحاباً ويتقاطر مطراً إن لم يكن البرد شديداً، وإن أصابه برد شديد فيجمد السحاب قبل تشكله بشكل القطرات نزل ثلجاً، أو بعد تشكله بذلك نزل برداً صغيراً مستديراً إن كان من سحاب بعيد لذوبان الزوايا بالحركة والاحتكاك، وإلا فكبيراً غير مستدير في الغالب، وإنما يكون البرد في هواء ربيعي أو خريفي لفرط التحليل في الصيفي والجمود في الشتوي، وقد لا يبلغ البخار المتصاعد الطبقة الزمهريرية، فإن كثر صار ضباباً، وإن قل وتكاثف ببرد الليل فإن انجمد نزل صقيعاً، وإلا فطلا، فنسبة الصقيع إلى الطل، نسبة الثلج إلى المطر، وقد يكون السحاب الماطر من بخار كثير يتكاثف بالبرد من غير أن يتصعد إلى الزمهريرية، لمانع مثل هبوب الرياح المانعة للأبخرة من التصاعد، أو الضاغطة إياها إلى الاجتماع، بسبب وقوف جبال قدام الرياح، ومثل ثقل الجزء المتقدم وبطء حركته، وقد يكون مع البخار المتصاعد دخان، فإذا ارتفعا معاً إلى الهواء البارد، وانعقد البخار سحاباً واحتبس الدخان فيه، فإن بقي الدخان على حرارته قصد الصعود، وإن برد قصد النزول وكيف كان فإنه يمزق السحاب تمزيقاً عنيفاً، فيحدث من تمزيقه ومصاكته صوت هو الرعد، ونارية لطيفة هي البرق، أو كثيفة هي الصاعقة، وقد يشتعل الدخان الغليظ بالوصول إلى كرة النار، كما يشاهد عند وصول دخان سراج منطفىء إلى سراج مشتعل فيسري فيه الاشتعال، فيرى كأنه كوكب انقض وهو الشهاب، وقد يكون الدخان لغلظه لا يشتعل، بل يحترق ويدوم فيه الاحتراق، فيبقى على صورة ذوابة أو ذنب أو حية أو حيوان له قرون وربما يقف تحت كوكب، ويدور مع النار بدوران الفلك إياها، وربما تظهر فيه علامات هائلة حمر وسود بحسب زيادة غلظ الدخان، وإذا لم ينقطع اتصال الدخان من الأرض، ونزل اشتعاله إلى الأرض يرى كأن تنيناً ينزل من السماء إلى الأرض وهو الحريق. قال (وقد تتكاثف الأدخنة) المتصاعدة بالبرد فتنزل بطبعها، وبرد حركة الفلك إياها عند وصولها إلى كرة
After finishing the discussion of simple bodies in two divisions, namely celestial and elemental, he began the two divisions of the discussion of compounds, namely those without mixture and those with mixture. He prioritized that because it is more similar to simple bodies from the aspect of lack of solidity of its composition and from the aspect of the permissibility of its being limited to two or three elements. He made it three types, because its occurrence is either above the earth, meaning in the air, or on the surface of the earth, or in the earth. The first type includes what is formed from vapor, and what is formed from smoke. Both are by heat, for heat dissolves from moist things aerial and watery parts which are vapor, and from dry things earthy parts mixed with fiery parts, and rarely devoid of aerial parts, and that is smoke. Then the ascending vapor may become subtle by the heat dissolving its watery parts, so it becomes air. It may reach the frigid zone and condense, gathering into clouds, and it drips as rain if the cold is not severe. If severe cold strikes it, the cloud freezes before forming into drops, it descends as snow; or after forming into drops, it descends as small round hail if from a distant cloud due to melting of edges by movement and friction, otherwise as large non-round hail mostly. Hail occurs in spring or autumn air due to excessive dissolution in summer and freezing in winter. The ascending vapor may not reach the frigid zone; if abundant, it becomes mist; if little and condensed by night cold, if it freezes it descends as frost, otherwise as dew. The ratio of frost to dew is like the ratio of snow to rain. The rain cloud may be from abundant vapor condensed by cold without ascending to the frigid zone, due to an obstacle such as winds that prevent vapors from ascending, or compress them into gathering, because of mountains standing before the winds, and like the weight of the preceding part and slowness of its movement. There may be smoke with the ascending vapor. When they both rise together to the cold air, and the vapor congeals into a cloud and the smoke is trapped inside it, if the smoke remains on its heat it intends to ascend, and if it cools it intends to descend. However it is, it tears the cloud violently, and from its tearing and friction occurs a sound which is thunder, and a gentle fiery thing which is lightning, or a dense one which is the thunderbolt. The thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, as observed when the smoke of an extinguished lamp reaches a burning lamp and combustion spreads into it, so it appears as if a star had fallen, which is the meteor. The smoke, due to its thickness, may not ignite but rather burn and its burning persists, so it remains in the form of a tassel, or a tail, or a snake, or an animal with horns. It may stop under a planet and rotate with the fire with the rotation of the sphere, and there may appear in it terrifying signs of red and black according to the increase in thickness of the smoke. If the connection of the smoke from the earth is not severed, and its burning descends to the earth, it appears as if a dragon descends from the sky to the earth, which is the fire. He said: 'And the smoke may condense,' ascending by cold, so it descends by its nature, and the cold of the movement of the sphere upon it when it reaches the sphere.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and causal explanation of atmospheric phenomena within the broader discussion of compounds (al-murakkabāt). The author first transitions from simple bodies (al-basā'iṭ) – celestial and elemental – to compounds, which he divides into two types: those without mixture (like meteors) and those with mixture (like minerals). He focuses on the first type because it resembles simple bodies in lacking solid composition and being limited to few elements. He then categorizes occurrences into three places: above earth (air), on earth's surface, and in earth. The first category (air) is further divided into those formed from vapor (bukhār) and those from smoke (dukhān). Both originate from heat acting on moisture and dryness. The explanation then proceeds sequentially: vapor rises, becomes subtle or reaches the frigid zone (al-ṭabaqah al-zamharīriyyah) to form clouds, rain, snow, hail, mist, dew, and frost, each with specific conditions. The author notes the ratios (e.g., frost:dew :: snow:rain). He then discusses clouds that form without reaching the frigid zone due to obstacles like winds and mountains. Next, he introduces the role of smoke mixed with vapor. When smoke is trapped in a cloud, its temperature determines movement, leading to violent tearing that produces thunder (ra'd) from friction, and either a gentle fire (lightning, barq) or dense fire (thunderbolt, ṣā'iqah). Thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire (kurat al-nār), creating a meteor (shihāb), or may burn persistently in shapes like a tassel, tail, snake, or horned animal, rotating with the sphere. Finally, if the smoke's connection to earth remains, its burning descends as a 'dragon' (ḥarīq). The passage concludes with a quotation from the original text (matn) stating that smoke may condense by cold and descend, with the sphere's cold movement affecting it, though this sentence is incomplete. The entire explanation is naturalistic, attributing phenomena to physical causes (heat, cold, condensation, friction) without explicit theological argument, but in the context of kalam, it serves to illustrate the order and wisdom in creation.

Key Terms
بخار (bukhār) – vapor
سحاب (saḥāb) – clouds
طبقة زمهريرية (al-ṭabaqah al-zamharīriyyah) – frigid zone
مطر (maṭar) – rain
ثلج (thalj) – snow
برد (barad) – hail
ضباب (ḍabāb) – mist
صقيع (ṣaqīʿ) – frost
طل (ṭall) – dew

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Weather phenomena are random or purely supernatural.
Correction: The passage shows that phenomena like rain, snow, and hail are systematically produced by natural causes such as heat, cold, and condensation, demonstrating order in creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what causes vapor to become air?
A) Contact with the frigid zone
B) Heat dissolving its watery parts
C) Condensation by cold
D) Friction with smoke

Key Takeaway
Vapor, through the interplay of heat and cold, explains a wide range of common weather phenomena in a systematic natural way.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that every drop of rain, every flake of snow follows a precise causal chain set by the Creator. When you see rain, does it remind you that nothing in your life is random—that your trials and blessings are also part of a wise decree? Will you start your next day by reflecting on a small blessing and acknowledging its ordered source?
?
The author explains that hail occurs only in spring or autumn, not in summer or winter extremes. This illustrates that even the most violent weather has a balanced cause. Is there an area of your life where you feel 'extreme'—anger, despair, or attachment—and you have dismissed it as uncontrollable? Will you trace that emotion back to its true cause and trust the One who balances all things to bring you moderation?
?
The vapor that becomes mist is the same substance that can become snow or rain—only the conditions differ. This means the same raw material can produce vastly different outcomes. What 'raw material'—time, energy, skills—has Allah given you that you are currently wasting on trivial matters? Will you change the 'conditions' of your heart (intention, discipline, gratitude) to transform that raw potential into something that brings lasting benefit?

Smoke-Based Phenomena: Thunder, Lightning, Meteors
Explanation of atmospheric phenomena caused by smoke, including thunder, lightning, thunderbolt, meteor, and other fiery shapes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how smoke trapped in clouds produces thunder, lightning, thunderbolts, and meteors, as well as other fiery atmospheric displays.

Core Idea
Smoke trapped in clouds generates violent tearing, producing thunder, lightning, and even meteors when ignited.

Building on the previous discussion of vapor, the author now turns to smoke (dukhān) as a second source of atmospheric phenomena. When smoke rises with vapor and gets trapped in clouds, its behavior—whether it stays hot or cools—leads to dramatic effects: thunder from tearing, lightning and thunderbolts from fire, and meteors from ignition. The author also describes unusual shapes and colors, linking them to varying smoke density.

Guided Path
1
Understand the interaction of smoke and vapor
Notice that smoke rises with vapor. When the vapor forms a cloud, the smoke gets trapped inside. The smoke's temperature determines whether it tries to rise or descend.
2
Trace the production of thunder and lightning
As smoke tears through the cloud violently, the friction and tearing produce a sound (thunder) and a fiery substance (lightning or thunderbolt) depending on its density.
The author distinguishes between gentle fire (lightning) and dense fire (thunderbolt).
3
Follow the fate of thick smoke
Thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, creating a meteor (shihāb). If it doesn't ignite, it burns persistently, taking various shapes like a tail or animal, and may rotate with the sphere.
The meteor is compared to the smoke of an extinguished lamp reaching a lit lamp.
4
Examine when the 'dragon' appears
If the smoke's connection to earth remains and its burning descends, it appears as a dragon descending from the sky—this is called 'al-ḥarīq' (the fire).
The passage ends with a quote about smoke condensing by cold, which is incomplete due to page break.

سحاب بعيد لذوبان الزوايا بالحركة والاحتكاك، وإلا فكبيراً غير مستدير في الغالب، وإنما يكون البرد في هواء ربيعي أو خريفي لفرط التحليل في الصيفي والجمود في الشتوي، وقد لا يبلغ البخار المتصاعد الطبقة الزمهريرية، فإن كثر صار ضباباً، وإن قل وتكاثف ببرد الليل فإن انجمد نزل صقيعاً، وإلا فطلا، فنسبة الصقيع إلى الطل، نسبة الثلج إلى المطر، وقد يكون السحاب الماطر من بخار كثير يتكاثف بالبرد من غير أن يتصعد إلى الزمهريرية، لمانع مثل هبوب الرياح المانعة للأبخرة من التصاعد، أو الضاغطة إياها إلى الاجتماع، بسبب وقوف جبال قدام الرياح، ومثل ثقل الجزء المتقدم وبطء حركته، وقد يكون مع البخار المتصاعد دخان، فإذا ارتفعا معاً إلى الهواء البارد، وانعقد البخار سحاباً واحتبس الدخان فيه، فإن بقي الدخان على حرارته قصد الصعود، وإن برد قصد النزول وكيف كان فإنه يمزق السحاب تمزيقاً عنيفاً، فيحدث من تمزيقه ومصاكته صوت هو الرعد، ونارية لطيفة هي البرق، أو كثيفة هي الصاعقة، وقد يشتعل الدخان الغليظ بالوصول إلى كرة النار، كما يشاهد عند وصول دخان سراج منطفىء إلى سراج مشتعل فيسري فيه الاشتعال، فيرى كأنه كوكب انقض وهو الشهاب، وقد يكون الدخان لغلظه لا يشتعل، بل يحترق ويدوم فيه الاحتراق، فيبقى على صورة ذوابة أو ذنب أو حية أو حيوان له قرون وربما يقف تحت كوكب، ويدور مع النار بدوران الفلك إياها، وربما تظهر فيه علامات هائلة حمر وسود بحسب زيادة غلظ الدخان، وإذا لم ينقطع اتصال الدخان من الأرض، ونزل اشتعاله إلى الأرض يرى كأن تنيناً ينزل من السماء إلى الأرض وهو الحريق. قال (وقد تتكاثف الأدخنة) المتصاعدة بالبرد فتنزل بطبعها، وبرد حركة الفلك إياها عند وصولها إلى كرة
After finishing the discussion of simple bodies in two divisions, namely celestial and elemental, he began the two divisions of the discussion of compounds, namely those without mixture and those with mixture. He prioritized that because it is more similar to simple bodies from the aspect of lack of solidity of its composition and from the aspect of the permissibility of its being limited to two or three elements. He made it three types, because its occurrence is either above the earth, meaning in the air, or on the surface of the earth, or in the earth. The first type includes what is formed from vapor, and what is formed from smoke. Both are by heat, for heat dissolves from moist things aerial and watery parts which are vapor, and from dry things earthy parts mixed with fiery parts, and rarely devoid of aerial parts, and that is smoke. Then the ascending vapor may become subtle by the heat dissolving its watery parts, so it becomes air. It may reach the frigid zone and condense, gathering into clouds, and it drips as rain if the cold is not severe. If severe cold strikes it, the cloud freezes before forming into drops, it descends as snow; or after forming into drops, it descends as small round hail if from a distant cloud due to melting of edges by movement and friction, otherwise as large non-round hail mostly. Hail occurs in spring or autumn air due to excessive dissolution in summer and freezing in winter. The ascending vapor may not reach the frigid zone; if abundant, it becomes mist; if little and condensed by night cold, if it freezes it descends as frost, otherwise as dew. The ratio of frost to dew is like the ratio of snow to rain. The rain cloud may be from abundant vapor condensed by cold without ascending to the frigid zone, due to an obstacle such as winds that prevent vapors from ascending, or compress them into gathering, because of mountains standing before the winds, and like the weight of the preceding part and slowness of its movement. There may be smoke with the ascending vapor. When they both rise together to the cold air, and the vapor congeals into a cloud and the smoke is trapped inside it, if the smoke remains on its heat it intends to ascend, and if it cools it intends to descend. However it is, it tears the cloud violently, and from its tearing and friction occurs a sound which is thunder, and a gentle fiery thing which is lightning, or a dense one which is the thunderbolt. The thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire, as observed when the smoke of an extinguished lamp reaches a burning lamp and combustion spreads into it, so it appears as if a star had fallen, which is the meteor. The smoke, due to its thickness, may not ignite but rather burn and its burning persists, so it remains in the form of a tassel, or a tail, or a snake, or an animal with horns. It may stop under a planet and rotate with the fire with the rotation of the sphere, and there may appear in it terrifying signs of red and black according to the increase in thickness of the smoke. If the connection of the smoke from the earth is not severed, and its burning descends to the earth, it appears as if a dragon descends from the sky to the earth, which is the fire. He said: 'And the smoke may condense,' ascending by cold, so it descends by its nature, and the cold of the movement of the sphere upon it when it reaches the sphere.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and causal explanation of atmospheric phenomena within the broader discussion of compounds (al-murakkabāt). The author first transitions from simple bodies (al-basā'iṭ) – celestial and elemental – to compounds, which he divides into two types: those without mixture (like meteors) and those with mixture (like minerals). He focuses on the first type because it resembles simple bodies in lacking solid composition and being limited to few elements. He then categorizes occurrences into three places: above earth (air), on earth's surface, and in earth. The first category (air) is further divided into those formed from vapor (bukhār) and those from smoke (dukhān). Both originate from heat acting on moisture and dryness. The explanation then proceeds sequentially: vapor rises, becomes subtle or reaches the frigid zone (al-ṭabaqah al-zamharīriyyah) to form clouds, rain, snow, hail, mist, dew, and frost, each with specific conditions. The author notes the ratios (e.g., frost:dew :: snow:rain). He then discusses clouds that form without reaching the frigid zone due to obstacles like winds and mountains. Next, he introduces the role of smoke mixed with vapor. When smoke is trapped in a cloud, its temperature determines movement, leading to violent tearing that produces thunder (ra'd) from friction, and either a gentle fire (lightning, barq) or dense fire (thunderbolt, ṣā'iqah). Thick smoke may ignite upon reaching the sphere of fire (kurat al-nār), creating a meteor (shihāb), or may burn persistently in shapes like a tassel, tail, snake, or horned animal, rotating with the sphere. Finally, if the smoke's connection to earth remains, its burning descends as a 'dragon' (ḥarīq). The passage concludes with a quotation from the original text (matn) stating that smoke may condense by cold and descend, with the sphere's cold movement affecting it, though this sentence is incomplete. The entire explanation is naturalistic, attributing phenomena to physical causes (heat, cold, condensation, friction) without explicit theological argument, but in the context of kalam, it serves to illustrate the order and wisdom in creation.

Key Terms
دخان (dukhān) – smoke
رعد (ra'd) – thunder
برق (barq) – lightning
صاعقة (ṣāʻiqah) – thunderbolt
شهاب (shihāb) – meteor
كرة النار (kurat al-nār) – sphere of fire
حريق (ḥarīq) – fire (dragon-like)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thunder, lightning, and meteors are supernatural events or caused by gods.
Correction: The author explains these as natural phenomena resulting from physical causes: smoke trapped in clouds, friction, heat, and ignition at the sphere of fire.

Checkpoint
What is the cause of thunder according to this passage?
A) Clouds colliding with mountains
B) The tearing and friction of smoke within the cloud
C) The melting of hail in the air
D) The ignition of smoke at the sphere of fire

Key Takeaway
Smoke trapped in clouds, through tearing and heating, gives rise to thunder, lightning, thunderbolts, and even meteors, all explained by natural causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever looked up at a lightning flash with a mix of awe and fear? Now that you know this natural cause—smoke tearing clouds—does your awe diminish or transform into wonder at the Creator's design? What might it mean for your faith if you see His hand in ordinary processes rather than only in miracles?
?
When you hear thunder or see a meteor, do you tend to interpret it as a sign or warning? This passage explains these phenomena as ordinary outcomes of smoke and fire. Why do you think many people still seek supernatural meanings in natural events? What would change in your daily awareness if you consistently saw all such events as part of Allah's natural order?
?
The passage compares a meteor to the smoke of an extinguished lamp igniting from another flame. In your life, where do you see small, 'lamp-like' causes leading to large, 'meteor-like' effects? Are you quick to attribute your successes or failures to dramatic causes rather than to the steady, cause-and-effect systems Allah has established? How can you train yourself to see His wisdom in the ordinary?

The Author's Observation of a Lunar Rainbow (Moonbow)
The author recounts his own observation of a colored moonbow in Turkestan, noting its differences from solar rainbows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rare natural phenomenon of a moonbow and its differences from a solar rainbow based on a firsthand account.

Core Idea
A moonbow is a rare, fainter cousin of the rainbow, observed by the author in Turkestan.

This passage presents a rare firsthand observation of a lunar rainbow, or moonbow. The author notes that while moonbows are typically colorless, he witnessed one with colors, albeit dimmer and less vibrant than a solar rainbow. This account offers a unique scientific observation from the medieval period, blending empirical reporting with the author's critical note on prior claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's own description come through.

2
Identify the author's correction
The author first states a common opinion, then presents his own contrary observation. Identify both parts.
3
Compare moonbow to solar rainbow
List the differences the author mentions: size, brightness, clarity, and color density.
The moonbow was smaller, denser in color, and less bright.
4
Note the observational conditions
The author describes the atmospheric conditions: moist air, thin clouds, moon near horizon. Reflect on why these conditions matter.
These conditions are typical for moonbow sightings.

Source Text

ذكروا أن القمر قد يحدث على الندرة قوساً خيالية، لا يكون لها ألوان، لكني قد شاهدت بتركستان في سنة ثلاث وستين وسبعمائة في خلاف جهة القمر قوساً على ألوان قوس قزح إلا أنها كانت أصغر منها كثيراً، وكانت بحيث تكاد تتم دائرة، ولم تكن ألوانها في ضياء صفاء الألوان الشمسية وإشراقها . بل أكثف، وكان ذلك في ليلة رشية الجو رقيقة السحاب، والقمر على قرب من الأفق.
They mentioned that the moon may rarely produce an imaginary arc, which has no colors, but I myself witnessed in Turkestan in the year 763 [AH] opposite the direction of the moon an arc with the colors of the rainbow, except that it was much smaller, and it was such that it almost formed a complete circle, and its colors were not in the brightness and clarity of solar colors and their radiance, but denser, and that was on a night with moist atmosphere and thin clouds, and the moon was near the horizon.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a personal observation by the author (or a quoted witness). It notes that the moon sometimes produces a faint, colorless arc, but the author saw a moonbow with rainbow colors, though smaller and less bright. The moonbow was almost a full circle, observed on a moist, thin-cloud night with the moon near the horizon. The colors were denser and less brilliant than solar rainbows.

Definition of Mizaj (Temperament) in Compound Bodies
Author defines mizaj and discusses its conditions and causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mizaj as an intermediate quality arising from the interaction of the four elemental qualities, and the conditions necessary for its occurrence.

Core Idea
Mizaj is a balanced quality that emerges from the mutual cancellation of extreme elemental qualities.

In this passage, the author turns to compound bodies that possess a temperament (mizaj). He begins with the introduction, defining mizaj as a uniform intermediate quality that results when the four elements, reduced to tiny parts, interact and break each other's intensity. This foundational concept sets the stage for understanding how mixtures and compound substances are formed in the natural world.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how mizaj is defined as an intermediate quality arising from the interaction of elements.
The author emphasizes that it is a 'متوسطة متشابهة' (intermediate and similar) quality.
2
Identify the conditions for mizaj
The author specifies that the elements must be very small in parts and that all four elements must be present. Understand why these conditions are necessary.
Smallness of parts increases contact surfaces; all four elements are needed because fire requires generation and corruption.
3
Examine the role of qualities vs. forms
The passage contrasts the views of physicians (qualities as agents) and philosophers (specific forms as agents) regarding the active powers in mixture. Consider how this relates to the definition of mizaj.
The author notes that qualities are intermediaries, not true agents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القسم الرابع في المركبات التي لها مزاج : وفيه مقدمة ومباحث . أما المقدمة ففي المزاج إذا اجتمعت العناصر المتصغرة الأجزاء جداً، فتفاعلت بقواها فانكسرت سورة كل من الكيفيات الأربع، حدثت كيفية متوسطة متشابهة في الكل هي المزاج، واعتبر تصغر الأجزاء، لأن تأثير الجسم وإن أمكن بدون المماسة، إلا أنه في الامتزاج بالمماسة، وهي تتكثر بتكثر السطوح التابع لتكثر الأجزاء، والمراد بالعناصر أربعتها، إذ لا مزاج عن البعض عند الجمهور، فلا بد من الكون والفساد ليحصل النار، وبالقوى الكيفيات عند الأطباء والصور النوعية عند الفلاسفة حيث أثبتوها ، وجعلوا الكيفيات واسطة في فعلها، لا فاعلة . لأن تفاعل الكيفيتين إن كان معاً كان الشيء مغلوباً عن شيء غالباً عليه، وإن كان على التعاقب كان المغلوب عن الشيء غالباً عليه، وبالعكس، وأرود مثله على توسط الكيفية. فإن أجيب . بأن المراد أن هذه معدات والمؤثر أمر مفارق موجباً كان ليكون
He said: The fourth division: On compound bodies that have temperament. It contains an introduction and investigations. As for the introduction, it is on temperament: When the elements with very small parts come together, and their powers interact, and the intensity of each of the four qualities breaks, an intermediate quality similar throughout arises, which is temperament. Smallness of parts is considered because the effect of a body, although possible without contact, yet in mixture it is through contact, and it multiplies with the multiplication of surfaces, which follows the multiplication of parts. What is meant by elements is all four, for there is no temperament from some of them according to the majority. Thus generation and corruption are necessary for fire to occur. And by powers [what is meant is] the qualities according to physicians, and the specific forms according to philosophers where they affirmed them. And they made the qualities intermediaries in their action, not agents. Because if the interaction of two qualities is simultaneous, the thing is overcome by something predominant over it; if [the interaction] is successive, the overcome [quality] is [overcome] by the thing predominant over it, and vice versa. And he gave a similar example regarding the intermediacy of the quality. If it is answered that what is meant is that these are preparations and the effective [cause] is a separate entity, being efficient, so that...
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth division of the work, which deals with compound bodies that have a temperament (mizaj). The author begins with a definition: when the four elements (fire, air, water, earth) – reduced to very small parts – come together, their respective qualities interact and break each other's intensity, resulting in an intermediate quality that is uniform throughout the whole mixture. This resultant quality is called mizaj. He explains that the smallness of parts is necessary because mixture requires contact, and contact increases with surface area, which increases with the number of parts. He notes that all four elements must be present, as the majority holds that no temperament arises from only some elements. The presence of fire, for instance, requires generation and corruption. He then contrasts the views of physicians, who attribute the active forces to the qualities (kayfiyyat), and philosophers, who attribute them to specific forms (suwar naw'iyya) – both groups regard the qualities as intermediaries, not true agents. He discusses the interaction of two qualities: if they act simultaneously, one dominates; if successively, the dominated one alternates. He mentions a similar example concerning the intermediacy of quality. Finally, an objection is anticipated: perhaps these are mere preparations, and the true effect comes from a separate, incorporeal agent (amr mufariq) that is efficient. The passage ends mid-sentence.

Key Terms
مزاج
عناصر
كيفيات
امتزاج
صور نوعية
أمر مفارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think mizaj is a simple intrinsic quality rather than a resultant from the interaction of multiple elemental qualities.
Correction: Mizaj is defined as an intermediate quality that arises only when the four elemental qualities interact and break each other's intensity; it is not a pre-existing simple quality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition is necessary for the occurrence of mizaj regarding the elements?
A) Only two of the four elements must be present.
B) All four elements must be present.
C) The elements must be in equal proportions.
D) The elements must be in their original large sizes.

Key Takeaway
Mizaj is a balanced quality that emerges from the interaction of all four elemental qualities under specific conditions.

Necessity of All Four Elements for Mizaj
The author argues that all four elements are required for mizaj and presents a dissenting view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical scholars argued that all four elements are necessary for the proper formation of temperament (mizaj), and the minority view that permits fewer elements under certain conditions.

Core Idea
Each of the four elements plays an indispensable role in creating the balanced mixture of mizaj.

In classical natural philosophy, the concept of mizaj (temperament) describes the balanced mixture of the four elements. Before we can discuss how temperaments affect living beings, we must first understand what makes a mixture 'tempered.' This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses a foundational question: can mizaj occur with fewer than four elements, or are all four required?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then read the English translation. Note the author's clear assertion about the necessity of all four elements.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'الكسر والانكسار' (breaking and counterbreaking) and 'الكيفية المتوسطة' (intermediate quality). Understand how each element contributes to these processes.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the example of a mixture of water and earth: why does it not produce mizaj unless rarefaction and heat are added? This illustrates the need for the other elements (air and fire).
Rarefaction implies air-like expansion; heat implies fire.
4
Understand the fiery part
The author explains that fire must be generated through 'كون وفساد' (generation and corruption) from ether, because fire does not naturally exist in the sublunar world without a compeller.
5
Examine the dissenting view
Some scholars allow mizaj from fewer elements when parts are extremely small and thoroughly mixed, leading to interaction and the intermediate quality. Compare this to the main view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لا بد في حدوث المزاج من العناصر الأربعة، لأن في كل منها فائدة، لا يتم بدونها الكسر والانكسار، وحدوث الكيفية المتوسطة المتشابهة، ولهذا يرى المركب من الماء والتراب لا تترتب عليه آثار الأمزجة إلا بعد تخلخل في الأجزاء، وحرارة فوق ما في الهواء، فعلى هذا لا بد في تحصيل الجزء الناري من الكون والفساد، إذ لا يترك من الأثير إلا بالقاسر ولا قاسر. وبعضهم على أنه يجوز حدوث المزاج من اجتماع بعض العناصر، فإنها إذا تصغرت أجزاؤها جداً واختلطت تفاعلت لا محالة وحدثت الكيفية المتوسطة.
Second: That in the occurrence of mizaj there is necessity of the four elements, because in each of them there is a benefit, without which the breaking and counterbreaking and the occurrence of the intermediate similar quality are not completed. For this reason, one sees that a composite of water and earth does not produce the effects of mizaj except after rarefaction of parts and heat above what is in air. So on this basis, to obtain the fiery part there must be generation and corruption, since it does not depart from the ether except by a compeller, and there is no compeller. And some hold that it is permissible for mizaj to occur from the combination of some elements, for when their parts become very small and mix, they inevitably interact and the intermediate quality occurs.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is the second point in the discussion of mizaj. It asserts that all four classical elements (fire, air, water, earth) are necessary for the generation of mizaj. Each element plays a role in the process of 'breaking and counterbreaking' (al-kasr wa al-inkisar) and the production of the intermediate uniform quality. An observation is given: a mixture of just water and earth does not yield temperaments unless there is rarefaction and heat. To get the fiery component, generation and corruption must occur, as fire is not naturally present in the lower world except through compulsion. However, some scholars allow mizaj to occur from fewer than four elements if the parts become extremely small and mix thoroughly, leading to interaction and the intermediate quality. This presents a minority view against the majority requirement of four elements.

Key Terms
العناصر الأربعة
الكسر والانكسار
الكيفية المتوسطة
الكون والفساد
الأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a mixture of only two or three elements can produce a proper temperament (mizaj) without any additional conditions.
Correction: The author argues that all four elements are necessary because each contributes to the breaking and counterbreaking and the intermediate quality. However, a minority view holds that if the parts are extremely small and mixed thoroughly, mizaj can occur with fewer elements.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the fiery part needed for mizaj, and how is it obtained?
A) Fire is naturally present in the lower world and mixes easily.
B) Fire must be generated through generation and corruption because it does not naturally leave the ether.
C) Fire is obtained by simply mixing water and earth.
D) The fiery part is not actually required for mizaj.

Key Takeaway
The formation of mizaj classically requires all four elements, though some allowed exceptions under extreme rarefaction and mixing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are you trying to achieve spiritual balance (mizaj) while neglecting one of the 'elements' of faith – like the 'fire' of passion or the 'earth' of humility? The author insists all four are indispensable. Which element have you excluded, and why?
?
The source says a mixture of just water and earth does not produce mizaj without rarefaction and heat. Is there an area of your character where you are relying only on softness (water) and stability (earth) but lacking the warmth of zeal or the breath of spirit? What would it take for you to introduce that missing element?
?
The dissenting view allows mizaj from fewer elements if the parts become extremely small. Are you settling for a reduced version of iman, thinking that minimal effort can produce a balanced soul? What does the author's requirement of all four elements demand from you today?

3 min
The Second Mizaj and Above: Gold as an Example
Defines second type of mizaj with gold example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of mizaj extends to compound mixtures like the temperament of gold, and what distinguishes a true temperamental quality from a mere mechanical combination.

Core Idea
Mizaj is not just for simple mixtures—it also applies to complex metals like gold.

This lesson clarifies a key distinction in the theory of mizaj (temperament). The author explains that the definition covers not only the first type of mixture but also more complex ones, such as the formation of gold from mercury and sulfur. The passage explains the mechanism of this mixture and emphasizes that the resulting quality is a genuine temperamental quality, not just a mechanical juxtaposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage includes a quote from the matn followed by the commentator's explanation.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that the definition of mizaj covers 'the second mizaj and above it.' Understand that this refers to more complex mixtures beyond the simple first type.
The example of gold from mercury and sulfur illustrates this.
3
Understand the condition
The passage presents a conditional: 'if we make the agent the forms of the simples... not the forms opposing the composites.' This highlights two possible interpretations of how mixture occurs.
The processes of pounding and sublimation (القرع والانبيق) reveal the forms of simples.
4
Grasp the clarification
The commentator adds that the resulting quality comes from mixing the minute parts of composites, emphasizing a true blend, not just a surface combination.
This reinforces that the second mizaj is a genuine temperamental quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ( ثم) التعريف يتناول المزاج الثاني فما فوقه كما في الذهب من الزئبق والكبريت، إن جعلنا الفاعل فيه صور البسائط على ما يظهر بالقرع والانبيق لا الصور المعارضة على المركبات باعداد الكيفية المزاجية. أعني الحاصل من امتزاج الأجزاء المتصغرة للمركبات، كمزاج الذهب
He said: 'Then the definition covers the second mizaj (temperament) and above it, as in gold from mercury and sulfur, if we make the agent in it the forms of the simples as appears by pounding and sublimation, not the forms opposing the composites by preparation of the temperamental quality.' I mean, that which results from the mixing of minute parts of composites, like the temperament of gold.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage clarifies the scope of the definition of mizaj (temperament). The author quotes the original text (matn) which states that the definition covers 'the second mizaj and above it,' meaning more complex mixtures. The example given is gold, which is formed from mercury and sulfur. The explanation notes that if we consider the active principle to be the forms of the simple elements (as seen through processes like pounding and sublimation), then the mixture is not merely a combination of opposing qualities but a true temperamental quality. The commentator then paraphrases: 'I mean, that which results from the mixing of minute parts of composites, like the temperament of gold.' This emphasizes that the second type of mizaj involves a genuine blending of components at a micro level, producing a new homogeneous quality, as opposed to a mere mechanical juxtaposition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mizaj only applies to simple mixtures like the four humors, and not to compound substances like metals.
Correction: This passage shows that mizaj also applies to complex substances (second mizaj and above), as exemplified by gold formed from mercury and sulfur. The mixture involves a genuine blending of minute parts, producing a new homogeneous quality.

Key Takeaway

?

?

Name one specific trial you have been avoiding because it would require real internal change, and commit to facing it this week.
?

Impossibility of Increasing Both Opposites Simultaneously
Refutation of deviation by opposite qualities simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why deviation from balanced temperament cannot involve an increase of two opposite qualities at the same time, and the subtle exception involving elemental masses.

Core Idea
Inclination toward one opposite precludes simultaneous increase in its opposite.

In the study of temperaments, one might wonder whether it is possible to deviate from balance by increasing both heat and cold together. The author addresses this question with a clear logical argument, showing it is impossible unless we shift our focus from qualities to quantities. This passage refutes a potential objection and clarifies a key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to see the author's concise style. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
Pay attention to the logical flow: the first sentence introduces the limitation to eight temperaments.
2
Understand the key argument
The author explains that deviation from balance toward heat means heat exceeds cold. Therefore, cold cannot simultaneously be in excess. Grasp this logical point.
This is the core refutation of the possibility of two opposite qualities increasing together.
3
Identify the objection being refuted
Some claimed that three qualities could increase, adding four new temperaments. The author shows this is contradictory unless we consider elemental masses rather than qualities.
The exception is a subtle but important nuance.
4
Apply the distinction between qualities and masses
Understand that the argument hinges on qualities (kayfiyyat) vs. elemental masses (ajram). When we talk about masses, it is possible to increase both hot and cold masses together, but not their qualities.
This distinction prevents misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (أو) كيفيتين غير متضادتين فينحصر في ثمانية. يشير إلى أنه لا يمكن الخروج عن الاعتدال بالحرارة والبرودة جميعاً أو بالرطوبة واليبوسة جميعاً، لأن الميل عن حاق الوسط إلى الحرارة مثلاً، معناه زيادة الحرارة على البرودة، فكيف يتصور مع ذلك زيادة البرودة على الحرارة؟ وبهذا يبطل ما قيل: يجوز الخروج بكيفيات ثلاث، فيزيد على الأقسام الثمانية أربعة أخرى هي المزاج الحار البارد والرطب أو اليابس، والمزاج الرطب اليابس الحار أو البارد. نعم يتصور ذلك لو اشترط في المعتدل تساوي أجرام العناصر أيضاً، بأن يزيد جرم الحار والبارد جميعاً أو الرطب واليابس جميعاً.
He said: '(Or) two non-opposing qualities, so it is limited to eight.' He indicates that it is not possible to exit from balance by heat and cold together, or by moisture and dryness together, because the inclination from the exact middle to heat, for example, means an increase of heat over cold; so how can one imagine with that an increase of cold over heat? And with this is refuted what was said: 'Exit by three qualities is permissible, so there would be added to the eight divisions four others: the hot-cold and moist or dry temperament, and the moist-dry and hot or cold temperament.' Yes, that can be imagined if in the balanced [temperament] equality of the elemental masses is also stipulated, such that the mass of hot and cold both increase, or that of moist and dry both increase.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage refutes a potential objection regarding the eight temperaments. The author first quotes the statement that deviation from balance occurs by two non-opposing qualities, limiting the number of complexions to eight. Then he explains that it is impossible to deviate from balance by both heat and cold at the same time, because deviation toward heat means heat exceeds cold, so cold cannot also be in excess. He uses this to refute a claim that three qualities could be simultaneously increased, which would add four more complexions (e.g., hot-cold-moist). He then notes an exception: if we stipulate equality of the elemental masses (ajram al-'anasir), then it is conceivable to increase both hot and cold masses together (or both moist and dry). This subtle point shows the argument is about qualities (kayfiyyat) rather than quantities (ajram).

Key Terms
كيفيتين غير متضادتين
اعتدال
حرارة
برودة
رطوبة
يبوسة
أجرام العناصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the refutation applies universally, making it impossible to increase both heat and cold in any way.
Correction: The refutation applies only to qualities (kayfiyyat), not to elemental masses (ajram). If we consider masses, it is possible to have both hot and cold masses increase together while maintaining balance.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that one cannot deviate from balance by both heat and cold simultaneously?
A) Because heat and cold are not qualities.
B) Because deviation toward heat means heat exceeds cold, so cold cannot also be in excess.
C) Because the number of temperaments would exceed eight.
D) Because it is impossible for two opposite qualities to exist together in any case.

Key Takeaway
Deviation from balance cannot involve increasing two opposite qualities simultaneously, but the same is possible for elemental masses if their equality is maintained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you desire two opposite outcomes (e.g., wanting both comfort and deep spiritual growth, which requires struggle). How does this lesson's principle—that you cannot increase both opposites simultaneously in terms of qualities—reveal an inner contradiction you must resolve? What first step will you take today to choose one or to seek a balanced increase in the underlying intention?
?
The passage distinguishes between qualities and masses: opposites can sometimes coexist if considered from a different perspective. In your own spiritual journey, are you confusing outward actions (qualities) with inward states (masses)? For instance, do you judge yourself based on visible deeds while ignoring the intention behind them? Commit to one act this week that aligns your inner intention with your outer action.
?
The author allows an exception when equality is maintained. Name one relationship or habit where you try to hold two opposing attitudes (e.g., being humble but also assertive) in a way that may be possible if they are balanced. What concrete change can you make to ensure this balance, and by when will you start?
